Gold Level criteria 
Climate Change Adaptation Benefit. The project will support 
communities to adapt to climate change by support for climate resilient 
agroforestry practices; adjustment of agricultural calendars, crop 
patterns and planting practices; diversification of sustainable livelihood 
options; watershed protection; fire prevention; integrated natural 
disaster management and prevention systems (e.g., early warning 
systems, monitoring protocols, and improved techniques and 
technologies). 
Exceptional Community Benefits. With the average income between 
2.5 and 3 million IDR per household (464,684 –   557,620 IDR per 
capita), an average household size of 5.38 and a national poverty 
threshold of 582,932 IDR per capita most households in the project 
zone fall below the regional poverty level. Thus, the project zone 
qualifies as a rural area in which more than 50% of households live 
under the national poverty line. The project will positively affect the 
wellbeing of up to 30,000 people living around the project area.  
Exceptional Biodiversity Benefits. The project fulfils criteria for both 
vulnerability and irreplaceability. The project area supports between 1.5 
and 2.3% of global population of the Critically Endangered Bornean 
Orangutan population, in addition to 23 other Critically Endangered, 
Endangered or Vulnerable species. All are likely to become locally 
extinct without the project’s intervention. Monitoring will demonstrate 
that threatened species continue to persist in the project area and 
measures will be taken to enhance populations where possible. 
Expected verification 
schedule 
Q4-2024 
Prepared by 
PT Mohairson Pawan Khatulisiwa (MPK)

2 PROJECT DETAILS 
2.1 Project Goals, Design and Long-Term Viability  
2.1.1 Summary Description of the Project (VCS, 3.2, 3.6, 3.10, 3.11, 3.13, 3.14; CCB, 
G1.2) 
The Mayas Project aims to protect and restore a 33,747 hectare area of peat swamp forest in 
Ketapang District, West Kalimantan Province, Republic of Indonesia. The Mayas project avoids 
GHG emissions from planned logging and conversion activities, while protecting forest from illegal 
logging, forest encroachment, forest and land fires and opening of canals or ditches. In addition, 
degraded areas will be restored through planting and canal blocking). 
At the time of the project’s intervention the area was in the process of being drained, selectively 
logged and partially converted to industrial tree plantation by the concession license-holding 
company PT. Mohairson Pawan Khatulistiwa (MPK) in line with a government approved 
management plan. This would have resulted in greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions averaging 
2,406,748 tCO2-e per year (84,236,212 tCO2e over the project lifetime), irreversible loss of 
globally important biodiversity and a negative impact on the livelihoods and wellbeing of people 
living around the project area. This imminent threat received global media attention.  
In 2017, Yayasan Inisiasi Alam Rehabilitasi Indonesia (YIARI), a non-profit organization based in 
the region and working to protect the area, began discussions with MPK, leading to an agreement 
in late 2018 to transfer the ownership of the company to a new investor. Under a new 
management MPK is now wholly committed to the long-term protection and restoration of the 
project area, with the full support of the Indonesian Government.  
MPK will fully protect the remaining peat forest areas (now renamed as The Mayas Project) and 
restore those areas already affected by fire, land clearance and drainage. In parallel the Mayas 
Project will work in close collaboration with the communities surrounding the area to ensure 
benefits derived from the project area (no permanent settlements exist within the project area) are 
used to improve rural livelihoods and wellbeing and to develop greater resilience to climate 
change. The project will also protect the globally important biodiversity of the project area, 
including a population of Critically Endangered Bornean Orangutan estimated to be between 813 
and 1,204 (around 2% of the global population) and significant populations of at least eight other 
cri  tically endangered or endangered species. 
The project is not located within a jurisdiction covered by a jurisdictional REDD+ program.

2.1.8 Other Entities Involved in the Project 
Organization name 
Yayasan Inisiasi Alam Rehabilitasi Indonesia (YIARI) 
Role in the project 
YIARI is a non-profit foundation with expertise in wildlife protection, 
biodiversity conservation and implementing sustainable community-led 
conservation strategies in the same region as the Mayas Project. 
In 2009 YIARI established the first rescue centre for orangutans in 
Ketapang, West Borneo. For more than ten years, YIARI has been 
working alongside communities and other local actors to raise awareness 
of the importance of natural ecosystems and for the preservation of 
natural ecosystems, particularly forest and swamp forest ecosystems 
home to orangutan and other endangered and critically endangered 
wildlife. YIARI will provide services to the Mayas Project including; 
community development and consultation, and communities facilitation for 
forest protection, and biodiversity conservation consultancy. 
Contact person 
Dr Karmele Llano Sanchez 
Title 
CEO

+62-251-8417712

sustainable and responsible natural resource management practices for 
the benefit of climate change, biodiversity and people. 
KNI has been involved with the Mayas Project since its inception, and 
provides technical assistance focusing on carbon accounting, MRV, 
hydrological restoration & revegetation

2.1.9 Project Ownership (VCS, 3.2, 3.7, 3.10; CCB, G5.8) 
MPK holds legal license to the entire project area through Decree of the Minister of Forestry 
Number: SK216/Menhut-II/2008 (See Section 2.5.8). A copy of this license is available to auditors 
on request. This license, together with prevailing legislation, grants MPK the right to generate and 
sell carbon credits and to implement all planned project activities. This corresponds to ‘Project 
ownership arising or granted under statute, regulation or decree by a competent authority’ as per 
VCS Standard 4.7. 
2.1.10 Project Start Date (VCS, 3.8) 
Project start date 
01-January-2019
Justification 
After a series of actions and negotiations, on 4
th
 December 2018 MPK 
signed a binding agreement with new investors. This led to the 
immediate cessation of the logging, conversion and drainage plans of 
the company under its previous owners, and so is the effective date on 
which the project implemented activities which led to generation of GHG 
emission and reductions or removals. As would be expected for a 
project that seeks to avoid planned deforestation, these activities were 
by their nature legal, political and financial. For ease of carbon 
accounting, and to avoid the small partial calendar year treatment in 

CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
16 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
2018, the start date was conservatively taken as being 1
st
 January 
2019. 
2.1.11 Benefits Assessment and Project Crediting Period (VCS, 3.9; CCB, G1.9) 
Crediting period 
The project crediting period runs from the project start date on 1
st
 
January 2019 until 9
th

2.1.14 Physical Parameters (CCB, G1.3) 
2.1.14.1 Topography 
The project area lies within a tropical coastal peatland with a low and flat topography. Based on 
available data the highest elevation of the peat dome, located in the eastern portion of the project 
area, is approximately 14 meters above mean sea level, while in a few isolated hills the elevation 
rises to a maximum of 83 meters above mean sea level (Figure 1). The area is generally level with 
the majority of slopes within the peatland not exceeding 0.6%. The greatest slopes reach around 
7% on the hill patches as shown in Figure 1.  

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
19 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
  
Figure 1. Digital Elevation Model of the project area (left) and slope distributions of the project area 
(right) 
2.1.14.2 Soil Types 
Based on data from the Regional Physical Planning Program for Transmigration (RePPProT) and 
direct surveys (see Annex 1 and Annex 2 of the Annexes), the project area is dominated by peat 
soils (about 32,729 hectares; 96%). Non-peat soils are concentrated near rivers and hill areas. 
Further details about peat soil distribution are given in Section 3.2.1.2. 
2.1.14.3 Climate 
The Project Area lies within the equatorial weather region of West Kalimantan, dominated by two 
rainfall peaks in April and December and a dry season typically between July and September. 
Based on data from Rahadi Oesman Meteorological Station in Ketapang (15 km from the project 
boundary; Figure 2) collected between 1987-2019, average annual rainfall is 3,476 mm/year. The 
lowest average monthly rainfall can be found in August (128 mm) and the highest is December 
(518 mm). Monthly average air temperature is relatively stable at around 27
o
C, while relative 
humidity averages around 85%. The timing and severity of the wet and dry season can also be 
affected by the El Niño Southern Oscillation (ENSO) or other climate variability, such as the Indian 
Ocean Dipole (IOD). El Niño events can cause decreased rainfall, while La-Nina or IOD events 
can cause the opposite effect. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
20 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
 
Figure 2. Average monthly rainfall and temperature for 1987-2019 period (source: Rahadi Osman 
Station, Ketapang)  
2.1.14.4 Hydrology 
The project area forms its own hydrological unit, comprised of numerous catchments draining to 
the Tulak and Pawan rivers, and numerous creeks to the Sukadana coastline. Water balance in 
the area is typical of tropical peat swamp forest, where 50-60% of annual rainfall is lost to 
evapotranspiration while 35-45% is lost through saturation-excess overland flow during the wet 
seasons. The balance of the water input (around 1-5%) is attributed to loss through groundwater 
flow and a proportion to refill and maintain aquifer storage. 
Typical tropical peat swamp forest, like the project area, is characterized by a shallow water table 
depth, with a yearly average around 20cm below soil surface. In dry seasons, when 
evapotranspiration exceeds rainfall, the water table depth may drop by 50-80cm below the soil 
surface, while in wet seasons, soil pores are quickly filled to saturation and saturation-excess flood 
and overland flow takes place. 
2.1.14.5 Vegetation and land use 
The project area lies within a tropical peat forest ecosystem. At the project start date in 2019, it is 
estimated that 94.9% of the project area (32,026 hectare) was identified as forest. Meanwhile, the 
remaining 5.1% of the project area (1,721 hectare) was non-forest swampland (Figure 3, 4 & 5, 
see also Table 18).  
A forest inventory conducted in 2020 confirmed that the peat swamp forest is composed of mixed 
tree and non-tree species. The tree species composition reflects the high vegetation diversity of 
peat-swamp forest with at least 104 tree species from 42 families found within the project area. 
Common species found in peat swamp forest including perepat darat (Combretocarpus 
rotundatus), jelutong (Dyera polyphylla), punak (Tertamerista glabra), malam-malam/kayu hitam 
(Diospyros sp.), resak (Vatica spp.), meranti rawa (Shorea sp.), balangeran (Shorea balangeran), 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
21 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
nyatoh (Palaquium spp.), kempas/benggeris (Kompassia malaccensis), and gemor (Alseodaphne 
coriácea). The complete list of tree species found in the project area is presented in Appendix 1. 
Lianas, palms, herbaceous vegetation, and other non-tree species grow abundantly on the forest 
floor (Figure 5). Commonly occurring palm species include asam payo (Eloidoxa conferta), palem 
merah (Cyrtoctachys lakka), and rattan (Calamus sp., Khortalsia sp.). Other herbaceous plants 
and sedges found in the project area peat forest are Rhapidophora spp., Cryptocoryne spp., 
Liparis spp., Freycinetia spp., Thoracostachyum spp. and Schleria spp.  
  
Figure 3. Catchments in the project area (left), Main vegetation types within the project area (right) 
Within the non-forest proportion of the project area (4.3%; Figure 3 & Figure 5) vegetation cover is 
dominated by ferns, and shrub species. Common fern species observed in field are Swordferns 
(Nephrelopsis spp.), Climbing Fern (Stenochlaena palustris), Pteridium spp., and Glechnium spp. 
Sedges and grass species such as Scleria purpurescens, Hymenachne acutiguma, and 
Cogongrass (Imperata cylindrical) are also found abundantly. Amid the dominance of fern and 
sedge species, some woody species are growing including Paperbarks (Melaleuca sp.), tumih 
(Combretocarpus rotundatus), Tetractomia tetranda, gerunggang (Cratoxylon arborescens), and 
Trema orientalis. These are characterized as pioneer tree species which can grow quickly after 
disturbances to the naturally-occurring vegetation (e.g., fire).  

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
22 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
  
Figure 4. Peat swamp forest dominating the project area (left), Condition of the understory 
vegetation in the project area peat forest (right)

forest in the project area (right) 
2.1.15  Social Parameters (VCS, 3.18; CCB, G1.3) 
Within the project area there are no permanent settlements. Surrounding the project area, with 
adjoining administrative boundaries, are 12 village communities supporting a population of 30,000 
people (see   
Ta b l e 46, Section 4). These village communities define the Project Zone. 
The 12 villages vary significantly in size from the smallest (Ulak Medang) of 667 people, to the 
largest (Kuala Tolak) of 4,280. Most villages are comprised of simple houses built from timber, 
brick and corrugated iron. Livelihood activities of communities within the Project Zone – for both 
subsistence and income generation – are highly dependent on natural resources. The most 
important natural resource-dependent livelihoods activities, both for subsistence and income 
generation, are: 
• Agriculture (mainly rice and vegetables) 
• Fishing and aquaculture (including added value processing of fish products) 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
23 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
• Non-timber forest product collection (also for medicinal and cultural purposes) 
• Hunting for subsistence and trade 
• Logging for subsistence and income generation 
The 12 villages in the project zone were established between 16 and approximately 100 years ago 
(Kuala Satong, Tanjung Pura respectively). The great majority (>99%) of villagers belong to the 
Melayu ethnic group and are all majority Muslim (>99%) with a small minority of Christians and 
Confucius followers (<1%). While Indonesia has not formally adopted the term ‘Indigenous 
Peoples’ pursuant to the United Nations Declaration on Indigenous Peoples (UNDrIP), these 
villages would likely be considered Indigenous Peoples under international law. Villages are often 
divided into sub-villages or hamlets (Dusun), for example Sukamaju is divided into Dusun Teratai, 
Dusun Bujang Garang and Dusun Kalimantang. Children have access to primary and secondary 
education and literacy levels among the younger generations are high (80-90%). About 1-2% of 
teenage girls drop out of school because of unplanned pregnancies. Access to healthcare is 
possible but is limited by the cost of treatments. For a detailed description of the socio-economic 
conditions within the project zone please refer to Section 4.1.1. 
2.1.16 Project Zone Map and Project Location (VCS, 3.11, 3.18; CCB, G1.4-7, G1.13, 
CM1.2, B1.2) 
The project is located in the sub-districts of Muara Pawan and Matan Hilir Utara, Ketapang district, 
West Kalimantan Province, Republic of Indonesia (110°02'30" - 110°15'20" East, 1°35'30" - 
1°48'00" South). See Figure 6.

2.1.17 Project Activities and Theory of Change (VCS, 3.6;   CCB, G1.8) 
The Mayas Project has developed a strategic plan to achieve its core objective of establishing a 
long-term sustainable landscape management model that ensures forest, biodiversity and climate 
are protected and appropriately managed through carbon finance, while silvicultural systems in 
already cleared areas of the concession and other livelihoods strategies provide local communities 
with meaningful opportunities to improve their wellbeing. This strategic plan, showing project 
objectives, activities, outcomes and performance indicators is shown in full as Appendix 2. Key 
activities are summarized here. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template

biodiversity

2.1.18 Sustainable Development Contributions (VCS, 3.17) 
Project activities that support and contribute to Sustainable Development Goals are shown below. 
These goals also reflect nationally stated sustainable development priorities, as mandated by 
Indonesia’s Presidential Regulation no. 59/2017 concerning the implementation of SDGs in 
Indonesia. 
Promoting sustainable agricultural practices through agricultural trainings (to increase yields with 
organic fertilizer production and plant-based pesticide production), demonstration plots, increasing 
crop diversity and supporting farmer groups in improving their practices. Farmers are growing rice, 
sugarcane, chilli, eggplant, shallots and fruit trees, rotating crops and most importantly clearing 
land without the use of fire, greatly minimising the environmental impact of agriculture. The project 
is supporting the establishment, formalisation and professionalization of small business units 
through loan access, allowing them to grow and improve profits. These practices improve food 
security, local wellbeing and alleviate poverty thus contributing to SDG1, SDG2, SDG3, SDG10 
and SDG13. 
The project promotes learning opportunities for some of the most disadvantaged households in 
target communities. Children are able to access formal education opportunities (supporting them 
to improve literacy skills), but also informal ones where they are able to learn computing, English 
language, public speaking and presentation skills, environmental knowledge and how to market 
themselves to future employers) thus contributing to SDG3, SDG4 and SDG10. 
The project has facilitated an all-female fire-fighting unit which patrols hotspots in the project zone 
during high-risk periods to catch and extinguish fires as quickly as possible. The team uses drones 
to monitor the area and promptly identify the location of fires. The project also has an all-female 
team of weavers who produce eco-pots for nurseries growing both agricultural and native tree 
seedlings (replacing more widely used plastic ones) made out of dried pandanus leaves. Women 
play an active role in all agricultural activities. Overall, the project proactively mainstreams gender 
and promotes leadership roles for women, especially in the development of sustainable livelihoods 
opportunities, thus contributing to SDG5 and SDG13. 
The project always targets the poorest households in each community, therefore reducing 
inequality and tackling poverty. This contributes to SDG1 and SDG10 
The project supports climate action through climate change mitigation and the avoidance of 
approximately 2 million tonnes CO2 emissions yearly, thus contributing to SDG13. 
The project protects hundreds of species and halts biodiversity loss (measured by comparing 
baseline biodiversity losses to with-project biodiversity maintenance and/or gains), thus 
contributing to SDG15. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
28 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
Partnership programs to strengthen and improve the livelihoods of communities around forests to 
support sustainable development through agroforestry programs, agricultural interest areas and 
other business support on community-owned land and other unproductive land contribute 
to SDG17.

2.1.20 Risks to the Project (CCB, G1.10) 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
29 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
Risks to the project’s climate benefits were assessed using the VCS NPRA tool (see Appendix 3). 
Results of this analysis, together with the associated mitigation measures, are recorded via the 
online NPRA tool. While primarily concerned with climate benefits, the results of the analysis are 
equally applicable to community and biodiversity benefits (for further details see Sections 4 & 5 
respectively). No additional risks have been identified at the project outset. 
2.1.21 Benefit Permanence (CCB, G1.11) 
The Mayas Project is implemented under a long-term concession licence with 34 years remaining 
under the initial term. Prior to the expiry of this licence the project will seek a further long-term 
extension in order to maintain the project activities and benefits beyond the initial crediting/licence 
period. In parallel, the project will continue to advocate to government the importance of fully 
protecting any remaining intact peat ecosystems to preserve their social, environmental and 
climate benefits. An alternative outcome to a further long-term extension would be the designation 
by government of the area as being fully legally protected. Furthermore, the community 
development program will seek to enhance the local economy in a sustainable way in order to 
demonstrate the long-term value obtained through the long-term protection of the peat forest 
ecosystem as opposed to its short-term destructive exploitation. As with the climate and 
environmental benefits, the project will continue to advocate for the recognition of this societal and 
long-term economic benefit. Through these measures the Mayas Project will seek to ensure the 
long-term permanence of the projects benefits beyond its initial lifetime. 
2.1.22 Financial Sustainability (CCB, G1.12) 
The Project has access to secure financing sufficient to implement all planned activities for the 
duration of the crediting period. Details can be provided to the auditors on request. 
2.2 Without-project Land Use Scenario and Additionality  
2.2.1 Conditions Prior to Project Initiation and Land Use Scenarios without the Project 
(VCS, 3.13; CCB, G2.1) 
Prior to the project’s initiation, the project area was legally designated and licenced for drainage, 
selective logging and conversion to industrial timber plantation, all based on a legally approved 
management plan. Work began in 2016-2018 to implement this plan through the construction of 
drainage canals (see Figure 8). As such, the baseline scenario adopted is a continuation of the 
conditions existing prior to the project’s initiation, albeit the project was able to stop further 
implementation of the planned activity at an early stage. Full details of the baseline selection, 
additionality assessment and legally authorized baseline activity are given below in Sections 3.1.4 
and 3.1.5. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
30 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
For further information on the Ecosystem Type, and present and prior environmental conditions of 
the project area (climate, hydrology, topography, soils, vegetation, and ecosystems of the project 
area) see Section 2.1.4 above. 
 
 
 
Figure 8. Drainage canal construction activities in MPK's concession, Jan-Feb 2017 
2.2.2 Most-Likely Scenario Justification (CCB, G2.1) 
See Section 2.2.1 above and Sections 3.1.4 and 3.1.5 below. 
2.2.3 Community and Biodiversity Additionality (CCB, G2.2) 
As described in the previous sections, the baseline scenario is derived from a clear management 
plan that was legally approved and already underway at the project outset. This allows us to 
predict with considerable certainty the likely impact on biodiversity and communities if the plan had 
not been halted by the project.  
Under the baseline scenario the natural forests of the Mayas Project area would have been 
drained, logged and in part replaced with mono-culture acacia and jabon plantations. The natural 
forest would have been damaged or destroyed completely and the peat ecosystem would have 
been degraded. The loss of natural tree diversity would have resulted in the local extinction of 
many animal and plant populations, including that of the Bornean Orangutan. Under the project, 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
31 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
these natural ecosystems will be fully protected. For further information see sections 5.1.1 and 
5.1.2.  
The impact on communities of the baseline scenario would also have been profound. Loss of the 
natural forest would have led to the loss of access to all non-timber forest products currently 
utilized by project-zone communities. Culturally important areas would have been lost. While 
regulations would compel a licence-holding plantation company to identify such areas and ensure 
protection and access, this practice is widely ignored or only partially implemented, putting such 
areas at risk. Under the baseline scenario social capital would have been at risk. The typical 
response to the arrival of a large commercial operation is the erosion of social cohesion as 
benefits and costs become unequally distributed and factions form. There likely would have been a 
lack of inclusive participation of communities and lack of transparency in decision-making, leading 
to an opportunity for elite capture in which dominant groups typically benefit disproportionately, 
while hindering the flow of benefits to the marginalized households. Increased immigration and 
competition for scarce resources further creates opportunities for conflict. Under the project 
scenario social capital will be enhanced by the project working with, and supporting, legitimate 
social institutions within project-zone communities. The decisions of such institutions will be 
respected and support delivered in line with their requirements, while great efforts will be made to 
ensure benefits are equitably distributed. The Mayas Project also aims to identify and reach poorer 
and marginalized communities (following Indonesian Government definitions) and community 
members through a variety of sustainable livelihoods initiatives. These are designed to lift the 
poorest out of poverty by engaging them in community-based business development, agroforestry, 
and sustainable non-timber forest product use. Similarly, under the baseline scenario, it is likely 
that commercial land-use designations would prevail over village-based claims. Commercial 
companies typically base their claims on centrally created tenure maps. Where conflicts exist the 
typical response is short-term payment at undervalued rates. The Mayas Project works with all 
project-zone communities to create spatially accurate maps that define the agreed extent of village 
land and the agreed boundary of the project area, as well as recognition of other spatially explicit 
landscape features.  
An often stated ‘benefit’ of commercial exploitation is said to be increased employment, however, 
there is little precedence among plantation companies for active community engagement in 
operations or decision-making, and a great deal of precedent for importing a work force from other 
regions. Any local employment opportunities are typically for unskilled manual labourers who are 
offered little or no opportunity to receive further training. The Mayas Project is primarily 
implemented at the village level, in collaboration with village communities. Opportunities for 
involvement range from participation in planning and mapping initiatives, to direct employment (in 
both junior and senior positions, on a full-time, part-time or casual basis), to participation in a 
range of thematic initiatives. The Mayas Project will also implement a comprehensive program of 
training and capacity building programme, linked to agro-biodiversity-based livelihoods, improving 
agroforestry practices on peat, expanding opportunities for aquaculture, initiating mangrove 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template

CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
restoration along the coastal portion of the project zone, improving financial management, fire 
management and wildlife monitoring among others. 
2.2.4 Benefits to be used as Offsets (CCB, G2.2) 
The Mayas project is not seeking to utilize any distinct community and biodiversity benefits as 
offsets in any other form. 
2.3 Safeguards and Stakeholder Engagement   
2.3.1 Stakeholder Identification (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB G1.5) 
The project has adopted a wide definition of stakeholders: all agents thought to be affected by the 
project (positively or negatively), including those by simple virtue of proximity. By far the most 
important group of identified stakeholders are the village communities adjacent to the project area. 
Twelve such village communities were identified, including all villages and hamlets which share 
administrative borders with the project area and/or were known to access resources within the 
project area. This information was verified through semi-structured interviews and using local 
knowledge and longstanding relationships with some of the communities. Stakeholder 
identification was conducted during social baseline surveys conducted between 2018 and 2021, 
including: 
1)   Participatory rural appraisals (PRAs), informal discussions, as well as semi-structured 
focus group discussions (FGDs).  
2)   Cross-checking of information obtained through PRAs and FGDs by engaging community 
members who did not participate in the formal discussions through informal dialogue. 
Table 2 lists all stakeholders identified by the project considered likely to be affected by the 
implementation of the Mayas Project. Local communities are further classified by livelihoods, as 
these are the most common unit of alliance in the local social context. Most community groups 
engage in multiple livelihood activities rather than depending on a single source of income, and 
thus typically belong to more than one group. No community groups have legal rights over the 
project area, but they may rely to a lesser or greater extent on the resources within the project 
area, hence their inclusion as stakeholders.

Natural and human-induced risks and mitigation measures are identified and quantified via the 
online NPRA tool. See Section 2.1.20 for more details. Risks to stakeholder participation are 
considered in the next section below (2.3.7). Measures taken to ensure the safety of employees 
and vulnerable groups is given in Sections 2.3.4, 2.3.18 and 2.3.19. See also Appendix 3. 
2.3.7 Community Costs, Risks, and Benefits (CCB, G3.2) 
The Mayas project will always strive to ensure consultation and collaboration with communities is 
conducted to the highest standards of integrity and in a timely, transparent and accessible manner. 
Many Mayas Project staff have long-standing relationships with the communities in the Project 
Zone through their prior work with YIARI. They uphold the highest standards of transparency when 
interacting with communities because maintaining the level of personal trust developed is 
paramount, but also because this is considered the best strategy for genuine engagement and 
long-term success of the project. 
In its community engagement strategy, the Mayas Project has therefore prioritised clarifying costs 
(with respect to upholding responsibilities, such as halting illegal hunting, logging activities and 
uncontrolled fire use in agricultural land-use clearance and other practices) and risks (the fact that 
halting these activities where necessary will impact some households in the short-term and that 
some of the livelihoods strategies might not yield immediate benefits as hoped), while emphasising 
the project’s strategy to ensuring those most in need of project benefits receive them. More 
specifically, where some villagers are highly dependent on logging or hunting, the Project will 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
37 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
endeavour to provide alternatives for those members of the communities that provide at least the 
same level of income and better working conditions. More generally, the Project will implement a 
broad agro-biodiversity-based livelihoods strategy and target the poorest and most disadvantaged 
households in target Project Zone communities. 
The Mayas Project is aware of the responsibility it is taking on to ensure no community members 
are negatively affected by Project activities and strives to empower local communities and equip 
them with the tools and knowledge necessary to lift themselves out of poverty to ensure 
livelihoods activities have the highest chance of being effective and sustainable in the long-term. 
Similarly, activities targeting community benefits have been and will continue to be designed to be 
managed in the future by the local communities themselves, without the need for intensive 
external interventions. The project striving to ensure communities are able to undertake and 
manage activities themselves is the most secure means of ensuring the activities will continue 
even after project’s lifetime. Finally, the project itself is anticipated to set an example of sustainable 
land use management in the region, leading to wider adoption of the practices it is pioneering. The 
project will continue to offer tours to government agencies, other non-profits and any other groups 
interested in learning about its activities in order to spread best practices and lessons learned 
throughout the region. In this way the Mayas Project is and will continue to contribute to a wider 
region managed more sustainably with respect to carbon emissions, biodiversity conservation and 
equitable development of local communities.  
Please also refer to section 2.3.10 on consultations. 
2.3.8 Information to Stakeholders on Validation and Verification Process (VCS, 3.18.6, 
3.19; CCB, G3.3) 
The schedule of the field visit will be made public by communicating this at least 3 weeks in 
advance, and there will be meetings in each of the target villages prior to this to explain why the 
audit is happening and what sorts of questions may be asked. Communication methods used are 
likely to include posters in community meeting rooms, announcements at routine community 
meetings and special sessions which will discuss the validation and verification process in more 
depth, explaining the opportunity this presents for communities to discuss positive and negative 
aspects of the project, raising questions and concerns.

other non-MPK staff to overcome language barriers if necessary.

2.3.10 Stakeholder Consultations (VCS, 3.18; CCB, G3.4) 
Since 2017 (and indeed prior to that date), the Mayas Project has conducted a series of 
stakeholder consultations, baseline social surveys and participatory mapping activities at the 
village level and beyond. These consultation meetings are detailed in full in Appendix 4Error! 
Reference source not found. including 166 meetings with over 2,000 participants. Through this 
process, the project has disseminated information on ecosystem services in the project area, 
planned activities, expected impacts from the project and management plans. Meeting minutes 
and attendance sheets are available upon request. During all consultations with communities, 
efforts have been made to ensure that straightforward and accurate information is provided as a 
basis for any decisions, including information on costs, risks and benefits (see Section 2.3.4 
above). A document explaining the project’s Stakeholder Consultation Strategy is available and 
describes the need to ensure any information is presented in a form that can be fully understood 
by participating community members and in a timely manner to allow due consideration, together 
with guidelines as to how that should be achieved. All project staff need to follow this document 
when holding a meeting with local communities. 
2.3.11 Continued Consultation and Adaptive Management (VCS, 3.18; CCB, G3.4) 
The Mayas Project applies the principle of Free, Prior and Informed Consent (FPIC) and 
recognises that this is an ongoing process without a firm end-point as it evolves alongside the 
dynamics of the local context. As such the Project will use several approaches to ensure 
communication and consultation remains active throughout: 
1. A village facilitator / focal point that reports progress, issues encountered, needs and 
wishes of vulnerable groups and wider community to MPK staff once per month. These 
reports will be filed in the project filing system and used to inform formal and less 
formal focus group discussions and other types of community meetings.  Once these 
points have been confirmed with the wider community, action points will be 
incorporated in the MPK socio-economic work plan. 
2. Monthly or bi-monthly visits from MPK staff to each target community to discuss a 
variety of project components including livelihoods strategy design, implementation and 
progress. Relevant and implementable feedback from visits will be incorporated in the 
MPK socio-economic work plan. 
3. A functional Grievance Redress Mechanism will be essential to maintaining 
communication channels open and ensuring community members feel their concerns 
and grievances are heard and addressed in reasonable timeframes. 
MPK management and implementation strategy is fully adaptable and based on the principle of 
empowering staff and managers and to reflect on practices and adapt and change as required in 
order to achieve objectives. This adaptive approach is then incorporated into the project wide 
planning and budgeting process such that no management practice or activity is fixed – all are 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
39 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
open to change if change is required, as long as it is in compliance with company policies (e.g., on 
health and safety, ethics and compliance) and supportive of furthering project objectives. 
2.3.12 Stakeholder Consultation Channels (CCB, G3.5) 
FPIC, participatory mapping, participatory rural appraisals, conflict resolution meetings, thematic 
Focus Group Discussions, awareness raising and general consultation meetings have all been 
carried out by YIARI and MPK staff in target communities, involving as wide representation from 
different stakeholder groups as possible, and when this was not possible, speaking to a smaller 
group of community representatives. Mayas Project staff are particularly aware of elite capture 
issues within communities and will always carry out inclusive consultations when these are 
possible and practical. Because of the Covid-19 pandemic there have been restrictions on 
numbers of people that could gather at any one time (maximum 30 with 1.5 meters social 
distancing), this has meant project staff have had to facilitate the same meeting multiple times with 
smaller groups of stakeholders and/or visit individual households. 
2.3.13 Stakeholder Participation in Decision-Making and Implementation (VCS, 3.18, 
3.19; CCB, G3.6) 
As well as considerations discussed in 2.3.8 and 2.3.9, the Mayas Project is aware of practicing 
culture and gender-sensitive approaches to participation in decision-making and implementation. 
From a cultural perspective, communities are quite homogenous, as almost 100% of them are 
ethnically Melayu and are practicing muslims. Over 99% of Project staff are Indonesian nationals 
with the majority also being practicing muslims and with in-depth understanding of the local 
cultural and socio-economic context. The staff employed to facilitate participatory mapping and 
participatory rural appraisals are from West Kalimantan and mostly Melayu themselves. This has 
reduced the risk of any cultural misunderstandings during data gathering, consultation and initial 
implementation of project activities. 
Project Mayas has ensured a gender-sensitive approach to participation by: 
1. Ensuring all villages are aware that the Project is taking a strong gender-sensitive 
approach, where the role of women in natural resource management and sustainable 
livelihoods is particularly valued and enhanced; 
2. Ensuring meetings relative to decision-making processes for the whole community 
include separate all-female sessions where facilitators are able to gather input from 
women only. This input must be reported back to the community as a whole;  
3. Ensuring at least 30% of consultation participants are female (targeting 50% in the 
future);  
4. Ensuring the establishment of women’s livelihood groups that support female 
leadership development; 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
40 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
5. Ensuring sustainable livelihoods initiatives are led by women in at least 50% of cases; 
and 
6. Ensuring project design and implementation benefits from the input of women’s groups 
heads. 
7. Promoting the success of local women in leading positive change for their local 
communities. 
2.3.14 Anti-Discrimination Assurance (VCS 3.19; CCB, G3.7) 
MPK has anti-discrimination policies which set out that their staff are to be aware and proactive in 
identifying and preventing any type of discrimination or harassment happening in the Mayas 
Project, including discrimination based on gender, race, religion, among others. These policies are 
set out in the MPK staff manual, Code of Conduct, and Anti-Harassment Policy which all staff 
receive training on and are available to be accessed by all staff with access to the company 
database system (see also Section 2.3.17 below, and the staff manual can be made available to 
auditors on request). If ever deemed necessary MPK will provide additional training to ensure all 
staff have a complete understanding of the policies and, their obligations under it.  
2.3.15 Feedback and Grievance Redress Procedure (VCS, 3.18.4; CCB, G3.8) 
The Mayas Project has developed a formal grievance and redress procedure to prevent and 
handle any conflicts with and among communities and other stakeholders which may arise during 
the implementation of project activities. 
One of the most important elements of the grievance redress procedure is to prevent potential 
conflicts before they arise. Such precautionary approaches include the implementation of FPIC-
based community consultations, participatory planning, regular communication and trust and 
relationship building between project staff and community members. This helps to identify 
underlying grievances well in advance and allow them to be addressed in a less formal way.  
If any grievances occur and are reported from the project-zone communities and/or other relevant 
stakeholders in the form of letters, short messages or verbal communication, MPK will respond to 
them by following the formal handling process as shown in Figure 9. All reported cases will be 
assessed to identify and verify the cause, actors and scale of grievances, and MPK’s team of 
experts will recommend resolution options based on the feedback from the stakeholders. The 
degree of intervention and process will depend on the nature of disputes, and MPK will continue to 
monitor the cases. The project believes that most if not all grievances will be resolved this way, 
because based on past experience bringing in a third party will put communities ill at ease and in 
some cases undermine the trust that has been built. We think this will reduce the likelihood of 
formal grievances being raised.  
However, if a grievance is not amicably resolved after this process, support will be sought from an 
external conflict resolution specialist or mediator. If this process is still unsuccessful in resolving 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
41 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
the complaint a request to an Ombudsman will be submitted for formal mediation and arbitration 
and will be subject to a hearing at which both disputing parties have the opportunity to testify. All 
cases will be referred and examined to the extent allowed by Indonesian laws and regulations of 
the relevant jurisdiction before decisions are made, and both parties are bound to satisfy the result 
of arbitration. 
 
Grievance redress procedure 
• There are 3 levels in resolving complaints or 
issues that arise. The duration for complaint 
resolution is <10 days at level 1, <21 days at 
level 2, and <60 days at level 3. 
•  Complaints can be expressed via email at 
info@ptmpk.com, by contacting the MPK Call 
Center at 0811-9341-795, or verbally in 
person. 
• The Communications and Public Relations 
department will respond to incoming 
messages by collecting information and 
completing the reporter's identity details 
according to the prepared form, and verifying 
with recordings or other documentation. 
•  Conducting internal meetings with relevant 
departments based on the Priority Decision 
Flow, first classifying the scope, gathering 
information related to the complaint, and 
reviewing documents together. 
• Communicating the agreement with the 
reporter; if both parties reach an agreement, 
the complaint resolution is considered 
complete. If no agreement is reached, the 
complaint moves to Level 2. 
• In Level 2 complaint resolution, the Board of 
Directors may also be involved in considering 
certain aspects related to the complaint 
resolution. It may also require a Conflict 
Resolution Specialist or a professional 
mediator at this level. 
• If an agreement is not reached, the 
complaint resolution escalates to level 3, 
where the ombudsman will be involved at 
this stage. 
 
The Grievance process is shown diagrammatically in Figure 11 below.

2.3.16 Accessibility of the Feedback and Grievance Redress Procedure (VCS, 3.19; 
CCB, G3.8) 
Communities will have both the village facilitator’s contact and that of the MPK Focal Point in the 
form of mobile phone numbers and email addresses. These contacts and expected timescale to 
receive a response will be publicised in communal spaces through poster displays. Contacting the 
village focal point (or the MPK focal point directly) to voice any concerns or complaints will be a 
fixed agenda item at all community Project Mayas meetings and announced at every opportunity 
by local village facilitators. 
Socialization of the Complaints Mechanism Procedure will be carried out for internal companies 
and stakeholders. Publication media can be in the form of posters and/or e-posters containing 
cellphone and email contacts, as well as a general description of the flow of resolving complaints 
or conflicts. This will become the Complaints Mechanism Publication Plan.

The project is committed to providing training opportunities which increase participation of 
community members and skills development in targeted, locally appropriate ways. The project is

2.3.18 Community Employment Opportunities (VCS, 3.19.13; CCB, G3.10) 
The Mayas Project invests in people living within the project zone, the wider region, and Indonesia 
as a whole. It provides employment opportunities irrespective of gender, age, social class or 
ethnicity and other factors. A particular emphasis is placed on project-zone communities because 
this type of engagement is fundamental to the long-term effectiveness of the project. Staff or 
contractors, whether employed on a long-term of short-term basis, are all entitled to employment 
terms based on similar types of work and working conditions in the area of employment. Open 
positions have been advertised in a variety of ways to reach a broad array of potential applicants. 
This includes online posting on job boards and through social media. Local facilitators and/or field 
staff will visit all villages to announce job vacancy opportunities for certain level, so that the village 
government has an opportunity to discuss the position’s requirements and qualifications. We 
expect about 50% of project field staff to be hired from project zone communities. All other project 
staff are from Indonesia. 
2.3.19 Occupational Safety Assessment (VCS, 3.19; CCB, G3.12) 
Worker safety and safe operations are a central priority of the Mayas Project, which conforms with 
the requirements of the labour law, UU No. 13/2003 and the provisions of Occupational Safety UU 
No. 01/1970, Government Regulation No. 50 of 2012 concerning the Implementation of the 
Occupational Safety and Health Management System (SMK3), Regulation of the Minister of 
Manpower No. 5 of 2018 concerning Occupational Safety and Health in the Work Environment, 
Regulation of the Minister of Manpower and Transmigration No. PER-08/MEN/VII/2010 concerning 
Personal Protective Equipment (PPE), Regulation of the Minister of Manpower No. 
PER.03/MEN/1998 concerning Procedures for Reporting and Examination of Work Accidents. 
MPK and its employees share a firm commitment to: 
a)   Pursue the goal of causing no harm to people; 
b)   Protect the environment; 
c)   Use material and energy efficiently in the provision of products and services; 
d)   Respect our neighbours and contribute to the societies in which we operate; 
e)   Deliver community benefits, biodiversity benefits, carbon offsets and other products and 
services consistent with these aims; 
f)    Promote best practice of HSSE in our sector; 
g)   Manage HSSE matters as any other critical business activity; and 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
44 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
h)   Promote a culture in which all staff of MPK (including contractor staff) understand fully this 
commitment. 
To this end, MPK has adopted an HSSE policy stating that it: 
a)   Has a systematic approach to HSSE management designed to ensure compliance with the 
applicable law and to achieve continuous performance improvement; 
b)   Sets targets for improvement and measures, appraises and reports performance; 
c)   Requires its contractors to comply with this policy whilst engaged by MPK; 
d)   Engages effectively with neighbours and impacted communities; 
e)   Includes HSSE performance in the appraisal of staff and rewards accordingly; and 
f)    Recognises its responsibility to respect human rights in all aspects of doing business. 
Human rights are fundamental to MPK’s core values of honesty, integrity and respect for 
people, communities and the environment. 
MPK’s human rights approach will be informed by the UN Guiding Principles on Business and 
Human Rights and applies to all of its employees, contractors and suppliers. 
With support from a third-party contractor, MPK will develop an HSSE Management System with 
standards that adequately address significant HSSE risks. MPK shall review its HSSE 
Management System and policies at least once a year and document the results. The HSSE 
Management System shall contain the following risk management controls: 
a)   Significant HSSE risks associated with project activities are assessed and controlled to 
levels as low as reasonably practicable. 
b)   Staff (including contractor staff) who have responsibilities for HSSE are competent and 
have the resources to perform their roles. 
c)   Changes to facilities, processes and organisations are managed to maintain risk controls. 
d)   Emergency response plans, including those for medical emergencies and environmental 
disasters (such as fires or flooding), are established and exercised on an annual basis, as 
a minimum, to maintain preparedness. 
e)   Incidents are investigated and analysed to identify improvements. 
f)    Regular reporting to the MPK Board that HSSE controls are being maintained and are 
effective. 
In implementing the above, MPK management will permit any staff (including contractor staff) to 
stop any work, or prevent any work from starting, where adequate controls of HSSE risks are 
found not to be in place. Further details on specific mitigation actions implemented by MPK to 
address HSSE risks are available on request. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
45 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
2.4 Management Capacity  
2.4.1 Project Governance Structures (CCB, G4.1) 
The project will be implemented and managed by the Indonesian company MPK with contractual 
support from its technical consultants YIARI, and PT Karbon Natura Indonesia (KNI). A simplified 
organisational structure diagram is shown below in Figure 10. A full detailed organogram is 
available to auditors on request. MPK is responsible for all project implementation, financial and 
compliance control. 
 
Figure 10. Organogram of MPK 
Management and governance of the Mayas Project incorporates the principle of an adaptive 
management cycle, as depicted (Figure 13) and described in more detail below. This governance 
model will be maintained for the project’s lifetime. 
Plan: The project has clearly defined objectives (Section 2.1.1) which are used by the 
management and field teams to define activities (2.1.17), and in turn to create detailed 
implementation plans. As part of this planning process project risks are also identified and 
evaluated (See Section 2.1.20, NPRA).  
Implement: As per the project implementation plan, the project activities are implemented by the 
management and field teams as described above in this section.  
Monitor: Each objective and activity have defined monitoring criteria (see Appendix 1: List of tree 
species identified during forest biomass survey in the Permanent Sampling Plot in 2020) which are 
used as performance indicators of the activity’s progress. Some key activities have inbuilt 
monitoring mechanisms, such as community consultation processes (see Section 2.3) 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
46 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
Evaluate: Project management meetings are held at routine intervals depending on the level of 
implementation, these feed up to quarterly coordination meetings between all core project partners 
where project progress is monitored against the implementation plan and the key performance 
indicators, at these monthly meetings the central risk register is also amended if required. Minutes 
are recorded at meetings to ensure any decisions are documented. At intervals which correspond 
to the production of project Monitoring Reports, any such cumulative changes to that date are then 
additionally capture in the Monitoring Report itself and updated NPRA score. 
Adjust: Where the need for change is highlighted by the above process this is documented as a 
management decision and incorporated into the project’s objectives, activities, implementation 
plan or monitoring plan as appropriate for the required adaptation.

2.4.2 Required Technical Skills (VCS, 3.19; CCB, G4.2) 
MPK and its partners form a highly experienced team with decades of combined professional 
experience in the design and implementation of the planned project activities. These include: 
• Project Management: The Project is being managed by MPK’s    executive professionals 
each with >10 years of managing forestry concession and restoration projects. 
• Forest Protection and Restoration: Daily Forest protection is conducted by MPK and YIARI 
which has expertise in peat swamp forest protection and conservation, supported by 
consultant experts with >10 years’ individual experience in peatland restoration and 
conservation. 
• Carbon accounting and monitoring: this role will be led by KNI, a highly-experienced 
privately-owned consulting company hired by MPK to provide carbon accounting and 
coordinating carbon monitoring activities, implemented in the field by MPK staff.  
• Community Development: this role will be led by YIARI which has >10 years’ experience 
working with communities in the Project Zone. 
• Biodiversity Conservation: this role is also led by YIARI as an NGO skilled at wildlife 
monitoring, illegal wildlife trade issues and human-wildlife conflict resolution.

2.4.6 Avoidance of Corruption and Other Unethical Behavior (VCS, 3.19; CCB, G4.3) 
MPK has strict ethics and compliance and anti-bribery and corruption policies and practices in 
place. These include (inter alia):  
(a) a system of financial controls for the detection and prevention of contravening actions such as 
bribes, 
(b) procedures to ensure compliance with all applicable anti-corruption laws and regulations,  
(c) guidelines on what are acceptable gifts and hospitality to be offered or accepted and set value 
limits for registration and approval,  
(d) rules and procedures to ensure that any actual, potential or perceived conflicts of interests and 
any actions required to mitigate it properly are fully recorded and tracked,  
(e) procedures to ensure that facilitation payments are not made,  
(f) compliance rules and procedures for relevant ethics and compliance risks, which will include a 
periodic assessment of the main ethics and compliance related risks to identify any additional risks 
requiring mitigation, and a system for retaining documents evidencing efforts to support the 
compliance commitment and programme elements,  
(g) antitrust rules for communication with competitors where applicable which are in line with 
industry practices, and  
(h) a data collection and archiving system that assures the protection of personal data. Core 
management staff have been trained in the implications and application of these policies and 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
48 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
procedures and will receive refresher trainings. Finally, MPK will establish a helpline, email 
address or similar mechanism and clearly communicate its availability to enable staff and third 
parties to report any suspected violations of the programme, with a clear policy of non-retaliation 
and investigation procedures for allegations. 
When contracting with third parties, MPK uses risk-based due diligence procedures to ensure that 
the integrity of such parties is assessed and, where necessary, addressed to the satisfaction of 
MPK. As such, all MPK contractors to some degree are required to undergo due diligence 
screenings to confirm that:  
(i)   the third party has processes in place to comply with applicable anti-bribery Laws and 
regulations and complies with MPK's standards;  
(ii) key individuals providing services are not on international sanctions lists;  
(iii) politically exposed individuals that are engaged in the business are identified and their 
suitability assessed. Copies of all documentation are available to auditors on request.  
MPK’s ethics, compliance, anti-bribery and corruption policies are communicated to all staff 
through the staff manual, while senior MPK staff will receive regular training courses on ethics and 
compliance delivered by internal experts which will cover Indonesian regulatory compliance as well 
as international best practice. Such training will provide guidance on the identification and 
mitigation of risks around bribery and corruption, facilitation payments, conflicts of interest, gifts 
and hospitality, engagement of government officials and money laundering.

2.5 Legal Status and Property Rights  
2.5.1 National and Local Laws (VCS, 3.1, 3.6. 3.7, 3.14, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, G5.6) 
The Mayas Project is, and will remain, in compliance with all national, regional and local laws and 
regulations in Indonesia relevant to project activities. A list of relevant national laws and 
regulations is presented in Appendix 5. 
 
2.5.2 Relevant Laws and Regulations Related to Worker’s Rights (VCS, 3.18.2; CCB, 
G3.11) 
The Mayas Project and MPK operate in full compliance with Indonesia’s labour laws and continue 
to strive to set an example of best practice with respect to employment terms, conditions and 
practices. Indonesian labour law is principally governed by the Labour Law 13 of 2003. This 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
49 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
represents the highest and most comprehensive set of regulations governing employment, 
including such issues as employment agreements, working hours, wages, paid leave, termination 
of employment, discrimination and grievance procedures. Below this is a raft of implementing 
legislation in the form of government regulations, presidential and ministerial decrees. 
Amongst many other things, the Staff Manual describes in detail the grievance process that any 
employees can take if they are unhappy with any term of their employment. If the issue cannot be 
resolved internally any employees can report their complaint directly to the local Manpower Office 
which can then address the complaint directly to the company, seek to assist a bipartite resolution, 
or enlist the assistance of an independent mediator to seek a tripartite resolution. To date no staff 
have initiated such grievance procedures, but the opportunity always remains open. 
In addition to requirements under the body of employment law, MPK is also fully compliant with 
Social Security Law. These laws require MPK to register all employees for Social Security (known 
as BPJS Ketenagakerjaan and BPJS Kesehatan) and to make payments on their behalf. All staff 
are issued membership cards to the scheme. 
2.5.3 Human Rights (VCS, 3.19)  
The project recognizes, respects and promotes the protection of Indigenous Peoples, Local 
Communities’ rights and customary rights holders. The project supports the United Nations 
Declaration on the Rights of Indigenous Peoples and ILO Convention 169 by implementing robust 
FPIC processes, employing traditional musyawarah (inclusive deliberation-style) consultation 
processes and by ensuring communities themselves co-design programmes and initiatives they 
know will meaningfully address their most pressing needs. The project promotes inclusive, locally-
led decision making processes, while providing opportunities for growth, capacity building and for 
women to participate, contribute and voice their needs, desires and aspirations.  
The project promotes and protects local values (see Section 2.5.4), respects peoples’ dignity and 
strives to ensure an adequate standard of living is reached by the most vulnerable households in 
the project’s target communities. 
The project will take a continuous improvement strategy and conduct socio-economic impact-
assessments at regular intervals adapting approaches to maximise positive impacts. 
2.5.4 Indigenous Peoples and Cultural Heritage (VCS, 3.18, 3.19) 
Around 99% of target communities in the Project Zone identify as Melayu (or Malay) people. 
Melayu are the second largest ethnic group in Indonesian Borneo after the Dayak and are well-
known for their strong Islamic identity. Some of the Malays living on Kalimantan coasts are the 
descendants of early Hindu-Javanese migrants from the era of the Majapahit Empire (around 600 
years ago), while others arrived with the establishment of Islamic Kingdoms or sultanates 
throughout Kalimantan (such as the Kingdoms of Banjarmasin, Kotawaringin, Pontianak, Kutai, etc 
500-200 years ago). Others arrived more recently from Riau (Sumatra) and the Malayan 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
50 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
peninsula. The Melayu people in the Project Zone largely fall into the latter group, sometimes 
called ‘contemporary Malays’. Some of the Melayu communities in the project area share cultural 
elements with the Srivijaya Kingdom in Sumatra. This can be observed in  their Keraton/palace 
buildings, sultanate tombs, use of local languages and traditional weaving culture. It is believed 
these communities established themselves sometime between 100 and 120 years ago. It is not 
known whether these communities were farmers originally; in West Kalimantan however they have 
mainly been dependent on collecting timber or becoming fishers both in rivers and along the coast.  
The project preserves and protects Melayu cultural heritage by supporting: 
• Weaving – women’s groups weave plant-based seedling pots used in mangrove and other 
tree planting activities. These pots are used in tree nurseries to replace the more 
commonly used plastic ones.  
• Aquaculture – the project supports small aquaculture farms. Aquaculture has long been 
practiced by these communities, but often the capital needed to start these small 
businesses is unavailable locally. 
• Islamic teachings about nature – the project works with imams and other religious figures 
to better communicate project goals and activities through language that draws on parallels 
with teachings in the Koran 
• Supporting traditional decision-making structures and processes – although the project 
supports women and other groups which historically have little or no decision-making 
power, it does it within existing structures and processes at the village level.  
2.5.5 Statutory and Customary Property Rights (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, G5.1) 
2.5.5.1 Statutory Rights 
The original license held by MPK is based on the Decree of the Minister of Forestry Number SK 
216 / Menhut-II / 2008 concerning the Granting of Business Permits for Utilization of Timber Forest 
Products in Natural Forest to MPK over a Production Forest Area of ~48,440 hectare in Ketapang, 
West Kalimantan Province. Minister of Environment and Forestry Decree No. 
SK.822/MenLHK/Setjen/HPL.0/9/2021 dated September 21, 2021, regarding the Amendment to 
the Minister of Forestry Decree No. SK.216/Menhut-II/2008 dated June 9, 2008, concerning the 
Granting of a Forest Timber Utilization Business License in Natural Forests to PT Mohairson 
Pawan Khatulistiwa for a Production Forest Area of approximately 48,440 hectares in West 
Kalimantan Province. Minister of Environment and Forestry Decree No. 
SK.960/MenLHK/Setjen/HPL.2/9/2022 dated September 5, 2022, regarding the Second 
Amendment to the Decree No. SK.216/Menhut-II/2008 dated June 9, 2008, concerning the 
Granting of a Forest Timber Utilization Business License in Natural Forests to PT Mohairson 
Pawan Khatulistiwa for a Production Forest Area of approximately 48,440 hectares in West 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
51 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
Kalimantan Province. In this decree, there is a change in the area size to 36,973 hectares due to 
portions being excluded as a result of the TORA (Agrarian Reform Object Land) area. 
MPK is currently proposing a Work Area Designation process as a result of the implementation of 
boundary demarcation which contains the location, boundary, area and boundary coordinate 
points listed in the Ministerial Decree.  
For the purpose of the AFOLU project, 33,747.28 hectares of the concession area make up the 
Project Area. The remaining 3,247.48 hectares have been excluded from the Project Area as they 
were allocated by the government for community partnership initiatives with MPK. MPK is still 
responsible for the entire concession area and will collaborate with community land users to 
implement sustainable land use management in line with the government’s request. 
In late-2018 MPK entered into agreement with new investors, a process completed in 2019. This 
led to new company objectives and so changes in how the company utilized its forestry license. 
The license is valid from 2008 to 2053 with possible multiple extensions under approval from 
MOEF (in units of 35 years). The original license granted rights for MPK to selectively log the 
remaining forested area and to establish timber plantations. Under its new management, MPK’s 
original forest utilization license was altered into a new forest rights scheme named Multi-Business 
Forest License, regulated under Government Regulation (Peraturan Pemerintah) Number 23/2021 
(“GR23/2021”) and Ministry of Environment and Forestry Decree no 8/2021. This regulation allows 
for multiple forestry business models to be implemented over the same license area.MPK is in an 
ongoing process for getting approval of the revised Multi-Business, which includes a license to 
utilize the license area for environmental services (including carbon), non-timber forest products 
and silvo-fishery. 
2.5.5.2 Customary Rights 
The majority of local community members use land outside of the Mayas Project Area. Two 
villages have settled inside the southern portion of the concession area (but outside the project 
area, as detailed in the previous section) and have been cultivating land for the past 1-2 decades. 
This part of the concession is excluded from the Project Area. The Mayas Project recognizes the 
dependencies of these three villages on this already cleared land and is committed to not 
resettling the community while finding suitable tenure arrangements. The Mayas Project will fully 
recognize customary rights and although not in a position to make these official (this land must 
remain part of the concession until legally excised by government) the project is forming an 
association to co-manage the land with communities and ensure resident communities are able to 
benefit from improved sustainable agricultural practices as much as possible, while supporting a 
reduction of fire use in this area. As a result, these areas are also likely to generate climate 
benefits but we have conservatively excluded them by not including them in the project area. 
In Indonesia all land is subdivided into village administrative units (the smallest administrative unit) 
including protected areas and concessions. Administratively, therefore the MPK concession 
overlaps with 12 village boundaries. In practical terms, village settlements are generally far from 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
52 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
village administrative borders, and any forest within those borders is of state property and 
managed by government authorities unless communities have obtained rights of management and 
use through one of a series of social forestry licenses. The project is committed to a detailed 
participatory mapping process to map community patterns of use and customary ownership 
precisely for each village. Social forestry licenses are usually granted over smaller areas of forest 
than those within administrative borders. The number of total hectares granted in a social forestry 
license depends on how high local population levels are and whether forest is close and easily 
monitored from settlements.  
Patterns of use, access points to timber and other resources in the Project Area are outlined in the 
HCV map (Figure 7).

2.5.7 Free, Prior and Informed Consent (VCS, 3.18; CCB, G5.2) 
Description of process for obtaining consent  
The Mayas Project will design and implement all project activities in participation with project-zone 
communities and based on full consultation and FPIC principles. The Mayas Project understands 
FPIC to be an on-going process, not one that reaches an end-point. As such, the Project commits 
to high levels of engagement, open communication and consultation to inform adaptive 
management and ensure meaningful benefits to participating communities throughout the duration 
of the project. 
Outcome of FPIC process  
The project has invested considerable resources, time and effort to create participatory maps that 
define the agreed extent of village land and the agreed boundary of the project area, as well as 
recognition of other spatially explicit landscape features. These maps also allow the project-zone 
communities to understand their spatial positions in relation to the project area, and to be able to 
plan future land use within their village boundaries without disputing other village territories or the 
project area. This tenure-based approach ensures that rights of the project-zone communities to 
lands, territories and natural resources are respected and protected.  
Mutual understanding of the goals and objectives of the Mayas Project between MPK and the 
project-zone communities is crucial for long-term success. To this end, and as part of the 
company’s commitment to FPIC and outreach activities having been conducted since 2017 2018 
MPK will be signing memoranda of understanding (MoU) with each of 12 village authorities in the 
project zone. This will likely happen following validation and verification, to avoid raising 
expectations unnecessarily. Each MoU is initially for a three-year period with opportunity for 
extension after review and evaluation by the village. These processes are shown graphically below 
in  Figure 11. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template

This project does not impact property rights, however it does adopt a policy of benefit sharing for 
affected stakeholders and local communities living in the proximity of the project. The project’s 
approach to benefit sharing is described in more detail in Section 4.5.6.  
2.5.9 Property Rights Protection (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, G5.3) 
As outlined in Section 2.5.5 no voluntary or involuntary removal or relocation will be implemented 
during this project. No activities important to culture or livelihood will be displaced. Local villagers 
who depend on illegal logging and hunting practices within the Project Area are a very small 
minority and will be incentivised to shift from unsustainable livelihoods to sustainable ones which 
will generate at least as much revenue and will provide better working conditions. This includes 
other community groups who will be supported to strengthen local governance, monetary and non-
monetary wellbeing. 
2.5.10 Illegal Activity Identification (VCS, 3.19; CCB, G5.4) 
Illegal activities that are either known to occur in the Project Area or Project Zone include:   

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
54 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
• Illegal sale of plots of land from within the southern portion of the MPK concession (outside of 
the project area, as described in Section 2.5.11 below) by members of two villages  
• Illegal logging within the boundaries of the MPK concession area with three distinct entry 
points (from the north and the eastern concession boundaries) 
• Illegal hunting of protected species 
In all three cases, community members involved in these activities are a very small minority. 
Measures to reduce these activities and ensure the risks to project success are minimised include: 
● Inclusive awareness raising efforts through community meetings and support in village 
level land-use and management plan drafting  
● Identification of loggers, hunters, and land speculators so as to be able to engage 
constructively and target their households for involvement in sustainable livelihoods options  
● Use community patrols, informants and guard posts to disincentivise the arrival of 
additional migrant loggers, hunters and land speculators once the resident ones have found other 
employment 
● The creation of village regulations to regulate the security of the village area 
Also refer to Section 2.5.4. 
2.5.11 Ongoing Disputes (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, G5.5) 
Within the Project Area there are no on-going disputes over land ownership or land rights. Outside 
of the project area, some communities in the southern portion of the concession (as described 
above) recognise that the land they use, legally belongs to the state and that management and 
use rights belong to the concession holder, but they wish to keep using and cultivating it. As 
described,1 the Mayas Project is collaborating with these two villages (Sukamaju and Sungai 
Awan Kiri) to co-create a management plan and livelihoods support strategies to improve local 
community members’ wellbeing, while reaching an agreement on eliminating the use of fire within 
concession boundaries and halting the illegal selling of plots of land to third parties. Halting the 
use of fire in this portion of the concession is both part of MPK’s responsibilities toward the 
national government and poses serious risks to the short and longer-term success of project 
activities.
