2.5.13 Double Counting and Participation under Other GHG Programs (VCS, 3.23; CCB 
G5.9) 
2.5.13.1 No Double Issuance 
The project is not receiving or seeking credit for reductions and removals from a project activity 
under another GHG program, or any other form of community,  or biodiversity unit or credit. 
2.5.13.2 Registration in Other GHG Programs 
The project is currently not registered or seeking registration under other GHG programs. 
However, as stated on Presidential Decree 98/2021 and Ministry of Forestry Decree no 71/2017 
and no 7/2023, the government of Indonesia, requires all carbon project in Indonesia to 
mandatorily register the project to Indonesian Registry System (SRN) and follow prevalent 
regulation pertaining to the Economic Valuation of Carbon (NEK). Registration in SRN means that 
the project opens a registry account, listed. produces a ‘DRAM’ (PDD), gets validated and the 
emission reduction is verified. As of monitoring period, MPK has not started registered to open an 
account. The project believes that the voluntary carbon market can contribute to the fulfilment of 
Indonesia’s NDC’s without double claiming the VCU/carbon credit.  
2.5.13.3 Projects Rejected by Other GHG Programs 
Has the project been rejected by any other GHG programs? 
  ☐   Yes    ☒     No 
The project has never been rejected by any other GHG program.  
2.5.14 Double Claiming, Other Forms of Credit, and Scope 3 Emissions (VCS, 3.24) 
2.5.14.1 No Double Claiming with Emissions Trading Programs or Binding Emission Limits 
Project reductions and removals or project activities will not be included in another emissions 
trading program or binding emission limit.  
The Mayas Project will never deliberately seek to double count emissions. The project will 
generate voluntary carbon units that will be retired to international parties which are not obliged to 
contribute to other country’s NDC. By doing this, the Indonesian Government does not need to 
make a Corresponding Adjustment to their reports of GHG reductions against the country’s 
Nationally Determined Contribution (NDC) target. As such, the carbon finance and implementation 
support generated by this project will help Indonesia meet its NDC target and thus support the 
achievement of the Paris Agreement. 
The credits generated by the project will be reported to the Indonesian authorities in line with 
obligations under Minister of Environment and Forestry Regulation 
P71/MENLHK/SETJEN/KUM1/12/2017, Presidential Decree no P98/2021 on Economic Valuation 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
58 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
of Carbon, Ministry of Environment and Forestry Decree No 21/2022 on the governance of 
Economic Valuation of Carbon, and any relevant future regulations. 
2.5.14.2 No Double Claiming with Other Forms of Environmental Credit  
The project activity has not sought, received, or is planning to receive credit from another GHG-
related environmental credit system.  
2.5.14.3 Supply Chain (Scope 3) Emissions  
The project activities do not affect the emissions footprint of any products, goods or services that 
are part of a supply chain. 
2.6 Additional Information Relevant to the Project  
2.6.1 Leakage Management (VCS, 3.11, 3.15) 
Not applicable. Under the Mayas Project, the project proponent, MPK is also the baseline agent, 
albeit under different management. The proponent/baseline agent only has control over resource 
use in the project area (via the concession license) and has no access to other forest resources or 
licenses. Based on this, leakage is therefore deemed zero. For further information see Section 
3.2.3Further Information  
Not applicable (no further information).

3.1.3 Project Boundary (VCS, 3.12) 
3.1.3.1 Spatial Boundary 
The project location, boundary and geographic coordinates are given above in Section 2.1.16. The 
spatially explicit sub-sets of this project area to which each methodology module is applied are 
given below in Table 4, Figure 12 and Figure 13. The location of project activities and 
infrastructure is described and mapped above in Section 2.1.16. 
The potential for sea-level rise to impact the project boundary has been assessed. Minimum soil 
surface elevation in the project area at the project start date is c.3 m-msl. Accounting for 0.3 – 1.3 
m sea level rise in 2100, the project area is predicted to be safe from future-anticipated sea 
encroachment under our management. Sea level rise impact on the drainage practice in the 
baseline scenario is also predicted not to be severe. With the rising sea level in the future, gravity 
drainage may become difficult during high tides, but this problem can practically be solved by 
implementing tidal drainage system; a technique that have been practiced by many plantations 
and in government agriculture programs in Indonesia since 1980. With these premises we 
considered that no further project boundary adjustment is required.

Baseline
 
Microbial 
decomposition 
of peat soils 
CO
2
 Yes 
Natural water tables of coastal peatlands of 
Indonesia are very shallow, close to the soil 
surface for most the year. Only native or plant 
species adapted to prolonged flooding or near-
saturation can survive in these conditions. 
Therefore, it has become standard practice by 
plantation companies or agricultural programs on 
peatlands to apply drainage to create more 
favourable conditions for dry-land plant species 
such as Oil Palm, Acacia and Jabon. 
Peatland drainage has been known to accelerate 
microbial decomposition of peat soils (see for 
example Hooijer, et.al. 2006). When the water 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
72 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
Source Gas Included? Justification/explanation 
table is lowered via drainage, the magnitude and 
duration of water saturation of the upper layer of 
peat soils is reduced, allowing more oxygen to 
enter soil pores. This facilitates microbial 
decomposition of the organic matter of peat soils; 
which releases various gases to the atmosphere, 
including CO
2
.

4

Recurrent fires have become a regular threat to 
the sustainability of coastal peatlands of Indonesia 
for decades. This anthropogenic impact is closely 
related to drainage. Fire has historically been 
present within the MPK concession boundary and 
the surrounding Project Area.  
Peat combustion, like any other aerobic 
combustion of organic matter, releases various 
gases to the atmosphere, among others CO2, 
CH4 and N2O.

3.1.4 Baseline Scenario (VCS, 3.13) 
See Section 3.1.5 below. 
3.1.5 Additionality (VCS, 3.14) 
3.1.5.1 Regulatory Surplus (VCS, 3.14) 
The Mayas project is located in a UNFCCC Non-Annex 1 country. The project activities are not 
mandated by any law, statute or other regulatory framework. 
3.1.5.2 Additionality Methods (VCS, 3.14) 
The project baseline (Section 3.1.4) and project additionality are considered in parallel using 
VT0001: Tool for the Demonstration and Assessment of Additionality in VCS Agriculture, Forestry 
and Other Land Use (AFOLU) Project Activities (Version 3.0). This tool is applicable for use with 
VM0007.  
Per VCS Standard V4.7, the baseline scenario represents the activities and GHG emissions that 
would occur in the absence of the project activity. In the case of the Mayas Project, the baseline 
scenario identified is planned selective logging and industrial timber plantation activities which 
were formally approved by the Government of Indonesia in 2016 and which were underway by 
early 2017. This baseline scenario and the additionality of the project activity were determined 
using VT0001 in the steps described in detail below. 
Step 1: Identification of alternative land use scenarios to the proposed VCS AFOLU project activity 
In this step, alternative land use scenarios that represent plausible baseline scenarios have been 
determined following the sub-steps: 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
74 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
Sub-step 1a: Identify credible alternative land use scenarios to the proposed VCS AFOLU project 
activity 
The list of scenarios below (in Table 8) is derived from existing land uses in the Project Area 
surroundings as well as an analysis of past and current trends and practices on peatland areas in 
Kalimantan and Indonesia, regardless of the tenure status of the land. From seven alternative 
scenarios, only one, the mining scenario, was dropped as no mining activity is present now or 
known historically in the project area.  
Table 8. List of credible land use scenario (Sub-step 1a) 
Scenarios Land use options 
Industrial tree 
plantation 
(status quo) 
Very common, huge expansion in early 2000s-2010s. In the approved 
management plan (RKU), c.60% of the area is designated for this use. 
Construction of core drainage canals began in 2016. See Figure 14. 
Commercial oil 
palm plantation 
Common on peat, but not on areas designated by government as forest 
estate (as is the case for the MPK concession). Most cases of oil palm 
plantations within the forest estate area involve communities or smallholders 
(see Sub-step 1b). Oil palm is more common on land excised from the forest 
estate (APL), or land earmarked for excise (HPK). See Figure 14. 
Selective 
logging (status 
quo) 
Much of the peat forests of Kalimantan were selectively logged in the 1970s-
90s including the Project Area. The original 2008 MPK license was issued as 
a logging license but was subsequently amended such that only c.40% was 
designated for selective logging. See Figure 14. 
Unprotected 
forest 
Such areas exist but are generally transitional; existing as a state between 
commercial exploitation and illegal local exploitation (logging, clearance / 
fire, then agriculture, typically by smallholders). Since 2011, most unlicensed 
(existing or pending) peat or forest areas have been placed under 
moratorium, which provides protection from legal planned commercial 
exploitation, but little practical protection from illegal local exploitation. 
Protected forest 
(project) 
As above, while some legal protection is provided through the moratorium 
and other regulations, these do not apply to areas that were already licenced 
or subject to a pending application in 2011. In theory the area could be 
designated as a national park or other similar protected area, but none have 
been created in the recent decade, so in all practical senses the area would 
be unprotected and vulnerable to unplanned degradation (logging, fire, 
drainage). NGO-based conservation projects have been suggested or 
created in some areas, but these are typically donor-based and therefore 
typically small-scale and short-lived. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
75 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
Scenarios Land use options 
Small-holder 
agriculture 
Common all over Kalimantan, but mostly in non-peat or shallow peat areas. 
Tends to be closer to access points such as roads, rivers/coasts (sand, 
mineral or shallow peat). Considered here due to prevalence in general. 
Often a strategy for illegal land speculation. 
Mining Some sandy ridges, heath forest and mineral soils area on the edge of 
peatlands occasionally have gold deposits which attracts miners, either legal 
or illegal. Mining activities are normally nomadic, once stock is depleted, 
miners will move to other areas. No known mining activity occurs within the 
project area.

Sub-step 1c. Selection of the baseline scenario 
Based on the above steps, a continuation of the existing pre-project land use of industrial tree 
plantation (c. 60% of the project area) and selective logging (c. 40% of the project area) is 
selected as the baseline scenario, corresponding to the approved management plan underway at 
the time. Two other scenarios, commercial oil palm and smallholder agriculture, are also retained 
for consideration under the barrier analysis (Step 3) as while not legally permissible, they are still 
prevalent due to ineffective law enforcement. 
Step 2. Investment analysis 
Investment analysis was performed in addition to Barrier Analysis (Step 3) for completeness, 
despite the tool proposing them as alternatives. 
Sub-step 2a. Determine appropriate analysis method 
Per the tool, as the VCS AFOLU project generates no financial or economic benefits other than 
VCS related income, the simple cost analysis approach was selected (Option I). 
Sub-step 2b: Apply simple cost analysis / benchmark analysis 
As demonstrated by project budget and financial forecasts made available to the auditors (see 
Sections 2.1.22and 2.4.5), the Mayas project has no source of revenue other than VCS related 
income. In comparison, the baseline activity is predicted to be a profitable venture, as 
demonstrated by documentation made available to the auditors as part of the NPV comparison 
presented in support of the Non-Permanence Risk Assessment (Internal Risk/Opportunity Cost).    
Step 3. Barrier analysis  
As stated above, four scenarios were entered into the barrier analysis: the existing pre-project land 
use of industrial tree plantation and selective logging (identified as the baseline scenario) together 
with commercial oil palm and smallholder agriculture.

Sub-step 3b. Show that the identified barriers would not prevent the implementation of at least one 
of the alternative land use scenarios (except the proposed project activity) 
Based on the analysis above (Sub-step 3b) both commercial oil palm plantation and smallholder 
agriculture are prevented by one or more identified barriers. Therefore, a continuation of the 
existing pre-project land use of industrial tree plantation (c. 60% of the project area) and selective 
logging (c. 40% of the project area) is identified as the project baseline. 
Step 4. Common practice analysis 
Effective management of intact peatland forest on land designated for production and held under 
license by an active plantation and logging company is not common practice in Indonesia. Outside 
of legally designated protected areas, and without the prospect of revenues from carbon finance, 
few examples exist. Those that do tend to be small projects backed by philanthropic donors, and 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
81 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
even in these cases, the projects can lead to conflict with local government or communities as the 
areas are perceived as making no financial contribution to the state, despite being designated for 
production. Other examples include other forms of offset projects whereby large corporates are 
paying management costs of the site as reparations for areas damaged as part of their operations 
elsewhere. These are rare and typically very small in extent. Based on this analysis the project 
activity is considered additional.

Per Ministerial Decree No. 65/2014, the timber harvesting applies a selected logging system with 
the diameter limit of 30 cm and a logging rotation of 40 years. Following the regulation, tree 
species listed under protection status are not logged. Timber harvest is restricted to commercial 
tree species that are classified into two main classes namely dipterocarp and ‘other commercial 
tree species’. Annual timber production must follow the Annual Allowable Cut (AAC) determined by 
the RKU, which is determined primarily based on a timber inventory and the logging rotation 
length. Based on the RKU, the Working Area under Management (Penataan Areal Kerja – PAK )  
and pre-logging inventory are carried out prior to logging. Infrastructure construction is done at the 
same time as logging. 
The RKU describes the use of a railway system (using the so-called “kuda-kuda” system; a 
traditional skidding and hauling technique widely used in peat forest systems) to transport logs 
from logging blocks to the main canal (Figure 17) and sets out the format for the railway network 
that is designed based on the layout of the logging blocks and canal network.

3.1.5.2.3 Baseline management of REDD-Avoiding Planned Deforestation (APD) area 
The conversion plan, as prescribed by the approved management plan, demonstrates there is a 
site-specific threat of deforestation in the project area. According to this document, the area to be 
managed as a timber plantation is 18,408 ha. However, in order to comply with existing 
regulations, 893 ha of the areas surrounding swamp is set aside for protection. Within the 
plantation area of 17,515 ha, some 884 Ha identified as Non-Forest. The remaining 16,631 ha of 
forested areas would be converted into a timber plantation. Referring to the RKU, Acacia timber 
(Acacia crassicarpa) will be processed further for construction-based products. Meanwhile for 
Jabon (Anthocephalus cadamba), the timber will be used for energy production. The spatial 
designation of plantation areas as prescribed by the RKU is shown by Figure 18. 
Of the 16,631 ha designated for timber plantation, 15,870 ha of forested areas will be converted 
into Acacia plantation and 357 ha for Jabon plantation. To support plantation operations, the 
remaining areas of 404 ha will be converted into canal/ infrastructures.   
The RKU details the method for forest conversion over a defined period of time that is then 
broken-down into annual forest clearance plans in the baseline scenario. Following the RKU’s

constructions, tree removals and plantings broken down into series of annual implementations.

Drainage and canal construction within the REDD area 
This project area is situated in one of coastal tropical peatlands of Indonesia and is characterized 
by its low elevation, flat topography, and high annual rainfall (c.a. 3,475 mm per year based on the 
Ketapang Airport Station record between 1987 - 2020); which is naturally subject to seasonal 
flooding, waterlogging, and shallow water tables. Only plant species adapted to such conditions 
(especially local species) can grow in this area. Unlike local species, primary plant species that 
would be planted in HTI stratum of this project area, i.e., Acacia crassicarpa, and Jabon, are 
introduced species that are intolerant to waterlogged soils and shallow water tables, thus require 
land reclamation (artificial drainage) to grow properly under such conditions.  
Land reclamations in Indonesia usually involve the application of surface drainage systems, 
consisting of a series of open channels including primary, secondary, and tertiary canals. Primary 
canals discharge drained water to natural water bodies such as rivers or the sea; secondary 
canals collect water from tertiary canals and deliver it to primary canals; and tertiary canals collect 
drained water from land and deliver it to the secondary canals. Primary canals need to be large 
enough (deep and wide) to achieve the required discharge capacity (usually ≥4 meters deep, ≥10 
meters wide). They also need to be low enough in the hydraulic head to collect drained water from 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
88 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
secondary canals. Similarly, secondary canals require certain physical dimensions to sufficiently 
collect water from tertiary canals and channel it to primary canals (usually ≥3 meters deep, ≥5 
meters wide). Physical dimensions of tertiary canals vary, usually 2 – 4 meters deep, 3 – 8 meters 
wide, depending on local conditions (variability of climate, soil and topography).  
 
Figure 19. Stratification map of the project area in the baseline scenario based on GHG emission 
characteristics: Water table depth strata (left) and drainage onsets (right) 
In the presence of drainage canals water tables would drop from an average 20 cm below soil 
surface (natural water table depth) to about 85 cm below soil surface. Spatial and temporal water 
table distributions vary based on climate, topography, drainage layout and characteristics, and 
physical soil properties. The deeper the drainage canals and freeboard (vertical distance between 
water surface in canal and surrounding land surface) the deeper water table depths would be. The 
baseline agent would maintain water table depths by maintaining water levels in drainage canals. 
In wet seasons, when excessive water input occurs, canals would discharge water to their full 
volumetric potential. In dry seasons, canal water levels may drop too low and cause extreme 
dryness in soils, posing a danger of wilting to plants. This can be prevented by installing water 
regulation structures, such as stop gates, to reduce outflow in dry seasons. 
Lowering water table depth increases the aerobic condition in the upper layer of peat soils, which 
in turn accelerates microbial oxidation in the upper layer of peat soils. This would be the main 
cause of GHG emissions from the drained peats soils in this project area. GHG release from upper 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
89 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
peat soils would also be attributed to mass loss of the peat soils. Gradual mass loss of peat soils 
over time manifests as subsidence, which eventually reduces the depth of the aerobic zone for 
plant roots. The baseline agent would overcome this problem by conducting regular dredging and 
restoring water table depth to previous levels. This process repeats in cycles, until the peat soils 
disappear or the drainage limit of the location is reached. 
Drainage and canal construction with logging/APDeg area 
Timber transportation in the baseline scenario would be conducted in two ways: railways and 
transportation canal, as shown in Figure 17. Railways can be implemented in areas of flat 
topography, but their construction on peat soils requires consideration of the soil’s physical 
properties, subsidence, local relief and climate. Peat soils are low in bearing capacity which 
means heavy loads may easily deform this type of soil. Engineers and practitioners would need to 
increase the bearing capacity of peat soils before and/or during railway construction. One way of 
achieving this is by inducing primary consolidation and shrinkage, achieved by constructing side 
canals at one side or both sides of railways. Excavated materials from the side canals can also be 
used to elevate the base of railways. Side canals can also act as interceptors for lateral overland 
flow that would otherwise flood the railways in wet seasons. Drainage canals for railway operation 
would eventually lead to GHG emissions and subsidence, and the baseline agent would overcome 
this by performing regular dredging. 
3.1.6 Methodology Deviations (VCS, 3.20) 
No methodology deviation is found in the application of VM0007 for the Mayas Project.  
3.2 Quantification of Estimated GHG Emission Reductions and Removals 
3.2.1 Baseline Emissions (VCS, 3.15) 
This section describes baseline emissions for each of the three spatially explicit project GHG 
accounting strata: APDeg, APD and WRC (see Section 3.1.3.1). This section starts by describing 
stratification of above ground vegetation, which is common to both APDeg and APD areas. This is 
followed by the stratification of below ground biomass, as applicable to WRC areas, and then a 
Project Area-wide assessment of baseline fire impact. Finally, baseline emissions are considered 
for each spatially explicit area in turn, following the corresponding methodology modules. 
3.2.1.1 Stratification of AGB 
Stratification of above ground biomass in the project followed VMD0016 (X- STR - Version 1.3). 
This module provides guidance on stratifying the project area into discrete, relatively 
homogeneous units to improve accuracy and precision of the carbon stock, carbon stock change 
and GHG emission estimates. Following X-STR when using remote sensing, the data was 
georeferenced into a common geodetic system. As mandated by the module, the project adopts 
GOFC-GOLD (2009) to apply best-practice methods in remote sensing.  

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
90 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
A land cover map derived from Sentinel 2 images was used as a proxy to stratify above ground 
biomass within the project area due to a strong correlation between land cover class and carbon 
density (per X-STR) that is required for the REDD-APD to quantify carbon stock and GHG 
emission from planned deforestation. Land cover also has correlation with timber volume (m
3
) that 
can be used for APDeg project to estimate timber potential and GHG emission from planned forest 
degradation. 
The stratification process comprised five stages: 1) data collection and pre-procession, 2) Forest-
non forest classification, 3) Re-classification of forest strata, 4) validation using field data, and 5) 
finalization of AGB stratification map.  Figure 20 illustrates the process followed to stratify AGB 
within project area.  
 
Figure 20. Flow chart showing the AGB stratification process

considered adequate to stratify the project area per band, as summarized in Table 16.

Pre-processing 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
92 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
Since Sentinel 2 images were used, these had already been corrected when the images were 
acquired and therefore did not require geometric correction. Radiometric and atmospheric 
correction was done in one step by assigning semi-automatic classification plug-ins. In running the 
pre-processing tool, the software performed "automatic computing" on the image so the haze and 
other atmospheric noises were reduced. A composite band tool was used to create a single raster 
dataset from multiple bands.  For the purpose of vegetation analysis, the band of 4,8, and 11 were 
used.   
Two images were required to fully cover the project area. Therefore, a mosaic was compiled to 
combine two images into one. This process also aimed to balance the digital properties of two 
images (hue, texture, brightness and colour) to avoid misinterpretation during the classification 
process. Following the mosaic construction process the images were ready for classification.  
 
Figure 21. The image before (left) and after (right) the processing 
Classification of Forest and Non-Forest  
Classification of forest and non-forest was undertaken following an unsupervised classification 
method. ARC-GIS 10.6 was used to automatically identify and cluster units with homogenous 
properties detected in the image. This process resulted a raster image containing 20 homogenous 
classes based on their digital signature (surface reflectance value).  
Using the result of the unsupervised classification, further analysis was undertaken to group the 20 
classes down to two classes: forest and non-forest, based on texture, color, hue, shape, and 
association. Figure 21 shows the initial classification result of forest and non-forest. This process 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
93 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
classified 32,026.29 hectares of the project area as forest and 1,720.98 hectares as non-forest 
(Figure 22).   
 
Figure 22. Raster image with homogenous classes derived from unsupervised classification (left), 
Initial analysis result from unsupervised classification (right) 
An accuracy assessment was then performed, per the methodological guidance of the module. 
125 random points were automatically assigned within project area representing both forest and 
non-forest. Using the high-resolution maps from 2016-2018 (Pleiades image with 0.5 m resolution, 
Spot Image with 1.5 m resolution, Google Earth Image) and applying a confusion matrix, the 
accuracy of the forest and non-forest map was identified as 93%, passing the threshold value 
accepted by GOFC-GOLD (2009).  
Re-classification of forest strata 
In the next stage of the analysis, the classification for the previous step was then to re-classified 
into forest strata derived from the previous step (Figure 22), applying NDVI (Normalized Difference 
Vegetation Index) as the basis for the analysis. NDVI is a common approach used stratify peatland 
forest which works by calculating the difference between near-infrared (which vegetation strongly 
reflects) and red light (which vegetation absorbs) using the following equation:  
NDVI = 
푁푁푁푁푁푁 −푁푁푅푅푅푅

By applying equation above, the ARC-GIS (using raster calculator tool) automatically identified 
NDVI values ranging from 0.245 – 0.784. With further classification process applying Natural 
Breaks and Class Breaks approach, three NDVI classes were identified (Figure 23). 
 
Figure 23. Forest and non-forest map of the project area based on visual interpretation (left), NDVI 
classes identified in the forest strata (right) 
Using the initial results from the NDVI classes as a basis, the image properties (i.e. texture, colour, 
hue, shape, and association) were used to delineate three forest classes. This process concluded 
that the forest strata of 32,026.29 hectares is comprised of three different classes, as shown in 
Figure 24 and Table 17.

Validation  
The project conducted a field survey to validate and finalize the forest strata determined through 
the remote sensing approach described above. A stratified random sampling grid was applied to 
determine the location of plots to be surveyed in order to validate (ground-truth) the forest strata. 
As suggested by VCS module, the project used Winrock’s calculator (adopting the CDM tool for 
the calculation of the minimum number of sample plots for CDM projects) to determine the number 
of plots that would be statistically representative of the three strata. Based on this, it was 
determined that 36 Permanent Sample Plots (PSP) within the project’s forested area would be 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
96 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
proportionally representative of the three forest strata (plot locations shown in Figure 25). In 
addition to this, the project also established 5 plots representing non-forest. The biomass survey 
was done by applying the Standard Operating Procedure /SOP (available in Annexes in Bahasa 
Indonesia) and the training was done prior to the survey implementation.   
 
Figure 25. PSPs distributed in the project area 
Finalization  
Data from field survey confirmed that forest type 1 is high-density forest, with an average C stock 
of 170.02 tC/ha. Meanwhile for the forest type 2 and 3, the field data strongly indicate medium and 
low-density forest with the C stock of 135 tC/ha and 53.83 C/ha respectively. Due to this 
insignificant difference (<5%), as suggested by X-STR module, and further confirmed by the 
statistical analysis, the forest type 1 and 2 were merged into one (average carbon stock: 147.11 
tC/ha). Combining the map with non-forest, the final AGB stratification map consists of the three 
strata shown in Figure 26 and Table 18.

Based on the averaged C stock per stratum derived from PSP data, total carbon stock stored 
within the living tree biomass in project area estimated to be 4,550,289.69 tC. From this amount, 
some 4,424,992.62 tC (97.25%) was stored in the forest stratum 1, 104,826.51 tC (2.30%) in the 
forest stratum 2, and 20,470.56 tC (0.45%) in the Non-Forest. 
3.2.1.2 Peat soil stratification 
Peat soils in the Project Area were stratified following field methods set out in section 5.3.2 of 
VMD0016. Data from direct field measurements (conducted in 2017 and 2019) and from 
secondary source (collected by FFI in 2016) were used to develop a peat soils distribution map. 
Sampling transects were arranged in such a way that each individual transect crosses the 
expected natural gradations/thickness contour lines of the peatland. More details on peat 
thickness modelling are described in Annex 2 of the Annexes, and the result is shown Figure 27 
below. 97 percent (32,729.8 hectares) of the project area was determined to be covered by peat 
deposits, with 93% (30,294.0 hectares) being at least 3 meters thick. Peat thickness distributions 
are summarized in Table 19 below.

3.2.1.3 Baseline Fire Modelling 
From an analysis of MODIS and VIIRS fire hotspot data (2007 - 2016) over a 1,017,780 hectare 
proxy area, a value of 4% was derived for annual percentage of area burnt (AAB%). Baseline fire 
events were then simulated using a spatially-distributed stochastic model (shown in Annex 4 the 
Annexes). As describe in Annnex 4, the project area was divided into parcels of about 1 hectare 
size. All drained parcels (depending on timing and land use scenario of individual parcels) have 
equal probability of being impacted by fire. Maximum burnt areas per year were set to be 
proportional to the total drained area of the corresponding year according the following formula: 
퐴퐴
푏푏푏푏푏푏푏푏  −퐵퐵퐵퐵퐵퐵,푡푡
 ≈ 퐴퐴퐴퐴퐵퐵%×��퐴퐴
푑푑푏푏푑푑 푖푖푏푏 −퐵퐵퐵퐵퐵퐵,푖푖,푗푗,푡푡
푁푁
푗푗=1
푀푀
푖푖=1
 
Where: 
Aburn-BSL,t     : Area burnt in baseline in year t (ha) 
Adrain-BSL,i,j,t  : Area drained in baseline in stratum i parcel j year t (ha) 
AAB%  : Annual percentage of area burnt (%) 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
101 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
 
Every fire incident in individual parcels was recorded during the baseline period. Every time the 
same parcel was burnt it added to its cumulative count, and the burn scar depth parameter value 
for the respective parcel was updated accordingly. Conservatively, no peat combustion was 
assumed to take place wherever and whenever the parcel water table depths were less than 0.4 
m-ss, or peat thickness was less than 0.2 meter. 
3.2.1.4 Baseline Avoiding Planned Degradation (APDeg) Emission Calculation 
The calculation of baseline emission resulted from commercial logging follows VMD 0006-BL-PL 
(estimation of baseline carbon stock changes and GHG emissions from planned deforestation / 
forest degradation and planned wetland degradation). However, since this module does not 
provide detailed calculations in the context of degradation through logging, the steps of IFM 
methodology (VM0010 -LtPF) were adopted. The parameters and data required to calculate 
baseline emission from logging are described below.     
Merchantable volume 
Using field data collected in February 2020, the mean volume of timber extracted per unit area 
(VEX,j,i) was calculated. Referring to the legalized planning document (RKU), all commercial trees 
with DBH greater than 30 cm were included. Meanwhile all protected tree species prohibited from 
logging were excluded from the calculation. The tree species were classified into two tree classes 
(j): dipterocarp and other commercial tree species. Table 20 summarizes the mean volume of 
timber extracted in the Project Area.

Carbon sequestered in wood products  
In all cases where wood is harvested for conversion to wood products, the carbon stock in the 
wood products pool (k) must be included in the baseline case. Based on discussions with experts 
and common practices in West Kalimantan province, dipterocarp tree species are typically used 
for sawn wood and other commercial trees for wood-based panels. In accordance with the VCS 
AFOLU Requirements, the amount of carbon stored in wood products that would decay within 
three years after harvest (i.e., the Wood Waste (WW) and the Short-Lived Fraction (SLF)), are 
assumed to be emitted at the time of harvest.  
Wood products that are retired between three and 100 years after harvest (i.e., the Additional 
Oxidized Fraction, OF), must be accounted according to a 20-year linear decay function. This 
decay function is applied when the net GHG emissions/removals are calculated on an annual 
basis in the equations below. All other wood product pools are considered to permanently store 
carbon. Therefore, the carbon stock of extracted timber that is immediately emitted to the 
atmosphere at the time of harvest is calculated as:

The equation above basically provides the general principle of carbon stock change/GHG 
emission calculation for logging/timber harvest. However, in order to accommodate the whole 
quantification process in the logging activity, some adjustments were made based on the carbon 
cycle of timber logging/harvest. Among other available IFM methodologies, the VCS methodology 
of Improved Forest Management-Logged to Protected Forest (VM0010) considered the most 
appropriate one for Mayas project as the project aimed to fully avoid the planned logging and 
conserve the forests.  The following are the main adjustments for the calculation of carbon stock 
change from commercial logging.  
As explained by Table 5 (selected carbon pools); the below ground tree biomass (CBBtree), above 
ground of non-tree biomass (CABnon-tree), below ground of non-tree biomass (CBBnon-tree), and 
the litters (CLI,) are excluded in the calculation.  
Baseline carbon stock change in aboveground tree biomass (∆퐶퐶
퐵퐵퐵퐵퐵퐵,푖푖,푡푡
) refers to the carbon stock 
emitted in the year of logging (CWPO).  
Baseline carbon stock change in dead wood (∆퐶퐶
푅푅퐷퐷
) includes the carbon stock change in timber 
from residual stand damage (CDWRSD) and carbon stock change of logging slash resulting from 
timber harvest (CDWSLASH). In the calculation, as prescribed by equation above, the Dead Wood 
emitted within 10 years with the decay function.  
Carbon stock entering the wood products pool at the time of degradation that is expected to be 
emitted over 100-years from stratum (CWP100) is emitted within 20 years.  
The baseline emission calculation of avoiding planned degradation refered to the plan of logging 
plan as prescribed by ten yearly working plan (RKU or Rencana Karya Umum). To be in line with 
the project start date, the emission from planned degradation under baseline scenario was 
calculated from 2019. 
Based on calculation, the net carbon stock change from commercial logging in the baseline 
scenario estimated to be 5,396,018  tCO2-e within the project period as shown by Table 23.

3.2.1.5 Baseline Avoiding Planned Deforestation (APD) GHG Emission Calculation  
The baseline emission calculation of avoiding planned deforestation referred to the plan of forest 
conversion into plantation (deforestation plan) as prescribed by ten yearly working plan (RKU or 
Rencana Karya Umum). To be in line with the project start date, the emission from planned 
deforestation under baseline scenario was calculated from 2019.  
For the purpose of GHG emissions calculations, the deforestation plan was overlaid with the AGB 
stratification map to produce the annual area of baseline planned deforestation for all strata and 
years (AAplanned,i,t), as detailed in Table 24.  
Table 24. The annual area of baseline planned deforestation for all strata i and year t

Post-deforestation carbon stock in above ground tree biomass in stratum 
i; t CO2e ha-1 
Per BL-PL, post-deforestation carbon stocks are the long-term average stocks on the land 
following deforestation. Since the plantation applies C stocks in a cycle, the mean stock across the 
cycle is used in the calculation. This gives the value of 31.61 tC/ha for Acacia plantation (8 years 
cycle) and 20.87 tC/ha for Jabon plantation (3 years cycle). 
The inclusion or exclusion of carbon pools for C stock and GHG calculation was analysed by using 
the tools T-SIG, BL-PL and REDD+ MF. Based on this, the calculation only includes aboveground

The GHG emissions in the baseline within the project boundary (GHGBSL,E,i.t) are conservatively 
excluded in this calculation. Based on this, the net GHG emissions in the baseline scenario from 
planned deforestation (∆CBSL,planned) are equal to the baseline carbon stock change in all 
terrestrial pools (∆CBSL,I,t).  
Based on these calculations, net carbon stock change in the baseline scenario from planned 
deforestation within the project area are estimated to be 5,433,647 tCO2-e as shown in Table 25.

GHGBSL-PEAT  : Net GHG emissions in the WRC baseline scenario on peatland up 
to year t* (tCO2e) 
GHGpeatsoil-BSL,i,t    : GHG emissions from the peat soil within the project boundary in the

GHGpeatburn-BSL,i,t  : GHG emissions from burning of peat in the base line scenario in 
stratum i at year t (t CO2e ha-1 yr-1)

the baseline scenario (ha)  
Apeatburn-BSL,i,t  : Area of peat burnt in stratum i in year t in the baseline scenario (ha)

Calculations were carried out in a spatially-explicit, dynamic fashion to ensuring the following 
restrictions in our model: 
Carbon stock depletion is consistent with subsidence 
Carbon stock is represented in tC/m2 as resulted from peat thickness (L in meter) multiplied by 
volumetric carbon density (Cvol in tC/m3) while carbon release rate (i.e., emissions) is in 
tCO2e/ha/yr. Stock depletion manifests as ΔL/Δt × Cvol hence the conversion from tCO2e/ha/yr to 
ΔL/Δt (i.e., subsidence) must be correct and consistent. 
Total carbon emissions do not exceed total carbon stock or total available stock for emission 
Carbon stock may be depleted after prolonged emissions. Once this happens carbon emissions 
from peat oxidation are no longer possible. Peat depletion manifests as subsidence. Every time 
land subsides the soil surface gets closer to the peat base. In many cases peat bases are lower 
than the minimum possible drainage depth (i.e., Drainability Elevation Limit, DEL a.k.a. drainage 
base). Carbon stock below DEL is not available for emissions, unless the responsible baseline 
agent is proven to be able and intent to apply mechanical pumping for years in order to maintain 
water table levels well below the DEL. In this project we conservatively assumed that the baseline 
agent is not able or willing to apply mechanical pumping in the future. 
For this calculation, the project area was partitioned into array of 100m by 100m (1 hectare) size 
parcels. At various locations parcels had to be further divided into smaller units in order to 
accommodate local features and variability such as canals and zone boundaries, depending on 
the corresponding designated land use stratum at the locations, i.e., based on baseline scenario 
map. Figure 29 below illustrates an array of square parcels further split by canals into smaller ones 
at certain locations.  
By applying this partition scheme, we have actually divided each stratum into smaller units, 
bringing spatially-explicit distributions into the calculations. To using these units, we introduced 
another spatial index j into equations for specifying individual parcels that appears in later 
calculations, in addition to existing index i for strata.  
.

Water table depth in parcels were inputed by referring to the designated average water table 
depths of the corresponding land use strata as given in Table 28. In order to achieve gradual water 
table transitions towards main canals, water table input values were weighted by the distance to, 
and the designed freeboard at, the nearest canals, taking into account long term climate 
characteristics, local topography and aquifer properties in the project area, as detailed in  Annex 5 
of the Annexes. Figure 30 provides a schematic of the vertical water table profiles within a 
plantation block in the baseline scenario.

A general rule was applied for every parcel that wherever and whenever water table drop below 
DEL, water table at that position was reset to DEL (i.e., the lowest possible water table at a 
position respective to its surrounding natural drainage). Descriptions of DEL is given in Annex 3 of 
the Annexes. 
Drainage and fires induce subsidence of the land surface. Subsidence attributed to drainage of 
peat soils comprises those associated with consolidation, shrinkage and peat oxidation. Fires also 
induce peat oxidation, but at a faster rate. Equivalent subsidence due to peat oxidation for each 
parcel was back-calculated using parameters bulk density and carbon content of peat soils upper 
layer using the following equation:

Equivalent subsidence due to peat combustion for each parcel was directly derived from burn scar 
depth. Burn scar depths were set to be dependent on parcels’ individual burning history as listed in 
Table 29.  
With the application of drainage in the baseline scenario (as also applies to existing drained areas 
in the project scenario) peat soils were expected to undergo rapid initial subsidence in few weeks 
to few months after first drainage, and is considered as one-time event. This rapid subsidence was

Baseline GHG Emissions from peat oxidation 
GHG emissions from peat soils in this project area are defined as CO2 emissions resulting from 
the oxidation of drained peat soils, omitting other potential gases, as set out in the GHG 
accounting boundaries. This project adopted a water table proxy (Hooijer, et.al., 2006) for this 
calculation, as given below:

Having a known water table depth for each parcel, CO2 emissions from peat oxidation were 
calculated by using a water table proxy. Peat oxidations were conservatively set to zero wherever 
and whenever water table has reached DEL or remaining peat thickness in the corresponding 
parcel became equal or less than 0.2 meters. Moreover, whenever and wherever parcel water 
table depth exceeded 1 meter, CO2 emissions from peat oxidation was capped to 90 tCO2-e/ha/y 
for the corresponding parcel.

3.2.2 Project Emissions (VCS, 3.15) 
3.2.2.1 Project emissions from REDD-APDeg area  
Project emission from APDeg area follows VMD0015 on the Methods for monitoring of GHG 
emissions and removals in REDD and CIW projects (M-REDD). The method specifically provides 
GHG removal or emission calculation from ongoing forest growth and forest disturbances 
(deforestation, forest degradation, and uncontrolled burning). GHG removal and emissions of the 
Mayas Project in the project scenario are described below.  
Project emissions as a result of deforestation 
With the project scenario it is assumed there is no deforestation occurring in APDeg area, 
therefore emission from deforestation set as zero.   
Project emissions as a result of forest degradation  
As remote sensing technology using optical sensors is not yet capable of direct measurements of 
biomass and changes, a ground-based method was adopted. A participatory rural appraisal (PRA) 
of the communities surrounding the project area shall be carried out to determine if there is a 
potential risk of illegal extraction of trees from the project area. If this assessment finds no 
potential pressure for these activities then illegal logging (ΔCP,DegW,I,t) can be assumed to be 
zero and no monitoring is needed. The PRA will be repeated every 2 years. 
If the results of the PRA suggest that there is a potential risk of illegal logging activities, then field 
sampling will be undertaken. The area that is potentially subject to degradation by logging 
(ADegW,i) uses the distance of illegal logging penetration from all access points (access buffer), 
such as roads and rivers or previously cleared areas, to the project area determined from the PRA 
with a width equal to the distance of degradation penetration.  

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
129 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
ADegW,i must be sampled by surveying several transects of known length and width across the 
access buffer area (equal in area to at least 1% of ADegW,i) to determine the presence or absence 
of new tree stumps.  
Where limited sampling provides evidence that trees are being removed in the buffer area, then 
systematic sampling will be implemented based on a detailed sampling plan. The sampling plan 
will be designed using plots systematically placed over the buffer zone so that they sample at least 
3% of the area of the buffer zone (ADegW,i). The diameter of all tree stumps will be measured and 
conservatively assumed to be the same as the DBH. The same allometric equation used for 
baseline carbon stock calculation would be used to estimate the carbon stock loss due to logging.  
The net carbon stock changes as a result of degradation is calculated as:

The Mayas Project is committed to implementing an intensive program to halt all illegal logging 
within the project area including through community development, ex-illegal logger employment 
and empowerment, and awareness raising. In this way, the project proponent is confident of 
halting all illegal logging within ten years of the project start date.  
The calculation of ex-ante GHG emission from illegal logging is based on the assumption that the

Net carbon stock change as a result of natural disturbances 
Uncontrolled burning is historically recorded in the project area, therefore must be anticipated in 
the project scenario. However, emissions resulting from uncontrolled burning is not reported in this 
document. Once the fire is occurred, this will be quantified and reported in the monitoring report.

3.2.2.3 Project WRC GHG emissions calculations 
Peatland Stratification in Project Scenario 
A distinct stratification could not be delineated for the project scenario, since water table depth 
distributions were modelled as continuous and gradual while also subject to changes in the 
position and timing of rewetting interventions. This was not considered a problem as actual 
calculations were carried out in a spatially explicit schematization (described later in detail) using 
over 50,000 spatial units of 1 hectare size or finer, which implicitly served as the actual strata. In 
summary, the same spatial strata used in the baseline scenario were applied without implying any 
attribution to peat emissions characteristics.

In the project scenario, the calculation method and spatial partitioning scheme of the project area 
were similar to those used in the baseline case, therefore many of the details are not repeated 
here ). For ex-ante calculations, water table depth distributions in the project area impacted by 
existing canals (i.e., parcels within a few hundred meters from existing canals, see Figure 32) 
were simulated using modelled values similar to those used for the HPH stratum in the baseline 
scenario, i.e., about 0.6 m average below soil surface. For the rest of the project area, water table 
depth distributions follow a pattern similar to that of the CZ (Conservation Zone) stratum in the 
baseline scenario, with approximate water table depth <0.22 m below the soil surface. Existing 
canals are planned to be blocked by 2023, and project emissions from peat soil oxidation are 
therefore expected to drop significantly from 2023 onwards. For ex-post calculations, water table 
depth distributions will be simulated using a hydrological modelling technique based on the actual 
water table, climate, and aquifer properties within the project area.

Project GHG emissions from peat fires 
GHG emissions from peat burning were assumed at zero in ex-ante calculations. For ex-post 
calculations actual emissions will be monitored. For each fire incident in the project area, all burn 
scar area will be mapped and their corresponding burn history will be tracked. GHG emissions 
from the combustion of peat soils in the project scenario were calculated using equation (3) in 
VMD0013 (E-BPB) as follows:

3.2.3 Leakage Emissions (VCS 2.5, 3.2, 3.6, 3.15, 4.3) 
Under the Mayas Project, the project proponent, MPK is also the baseline agent, albeit under 
different management. The proponent/baseline agent only has control over resource use in the 
project area (via the concession license) and has no access to other forest resources or licenses. 
Based on this, leakage is therefore deemed zero

An uncertainty analysis was performed in accordance with the X-UNC module. The outcome of 
this analysis is an overall estimate of the uncertainty of the total net GHG emission reductions 
from REDD and WRC project activities. The allowable uncertainty under X-UNC module is +/- 15% 
of Total Estimated Emission Reduction at the 95% confidence level. Where this precision level is 
met, no deduction is required for uncertainty. Where uncertainty exceeds 15% of the Total 
Estimated Emission Reduction at the 95% confidence level, the deduction shall be equal to the 
amount that the uncertainty exceeds the allowable level. Following the calculations (see Annex 8 
for detail of uncertainty calculation), the uncertainty was found to be below 15%, therefore no 
deduction was required. 
3.3 Monitoring 
3.3.1 Data and Parameters Available at Validation (VCS, 3.16) 
The Data and Parameters available at Validation for REDD are presented in Appendix 6: Data and 
Parameters Available at Validation for REDD (APDeg and APD) 
The Data and Parameters available at Validation for WRC are presented in Appendix 7: Data and 
Parameters Available at Validation for WRC 
3.3.2 Data and Parameters Monitored (VCS, 3.16) 
The Data and Parameters Monitored for REDD are presented in Appendix 8: Data and Parameters 
Available during Monitoring for REDD (APDeg-APD). 
The Data and Parameters Monitored for WRC are presented in Appendix 9: Data and Parameters 
Available during Monitoring for WRC 
3.3.3 Monitoring Plan (VCS, 3.16, 3.20) 
3.3.3.1 Monitoring Plan of REDD (APDeg-APD)  
Monitoring areas undergoing carbon stock enhancement  
The Mayas Project contains forest areas that are both deforested in the baseline and assumed to 
be accumulating carbon in the project scenario. Geographic boundaries of the areas are identified 
to monitor any changes that occur in the forest strata
 Ten (10) yearly ground measurements will be 
carried out in all Permanent Sampling Plot that were established in field for the baseline renewal 
purpose. The same allometric equation (used by BSL biomass) will be used to generate biomass 
for monitoring. Using this data, the calculation of carbon stock enhancement will follow the 
procedures of VMD0015. 
Monitoring forest degradation  

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
157 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
As prescribed by VMD0015, the Mayas Project will perform a participatory rural appraisal (PRA) of 
the communities inside and surrounding the project area to determine if there is the potential for 
illegal logging to occur. If the results of the PRA suggest that there is a potential for degradation 
activities, then limited field sampling will be carried out.  
The area that is potentially subject to degradation firstly needs to be delineated (A
Deg,i
). An output 
of the PRA will be a distance of degradation penetration from all access points (access buffer), 
such as roads and rivers or previously cleared areas, to the project area. The area subject to 
degradation shall be delineated (A
DegW,i
) based on an access buffer from all access points, such as 
roads and rivers or previously cleared areas, to the project area, with a width equal to the distance 
of degradation penetration. A
DegW,i 
shall be sampled by surveying several transects of known 
length and width across the access-buffer area (equal in area to at least 1% of A
DegW,i 
) to check 
whether new tree stumps are evident or not.  
If the limited sampling finds trees being removed in the buffer area, then a more systematic 
sampling will be implemented. The sampling plan will be designed using plots systematically 
placed over the buffer zone so that they sample at least 3% of the area of the buffer zone (A
DegW,i
). 
The diameter of all tree stumps will be measured and conservatively assumed to be the same as 
the DBH. 
The mean above ground carbon stock of the harvested trees will be conservatively estimated to be 
the total emissions and to all enter the atmosphere. This sampling procedure will be repeated 
every five years and the results annualized by dividing the total emissions by five. 
Monitoring deforestation 
The Mayas Project will collect satellite images regularly as the basis for monitoring deforestation. 
Pre-processing of the images will comprise geometric correction, geo-referencing, and cloud and 
shadow detection and removal. Guidance for the interpretation of remote sensing imagery is given 
in the GOFC-GOLD Sourcebook for REDD (2016) will be followed to map and calculate 
deforestation within project area. 
Monitoring burnt areas  
For every fire incident, the Mayas Project will map out the areas impacted by fire or burn scars. 
Pre delineation of burn scars shall be firstly spatially assessed using satellite images and hotspot 
data, mainly data from MODIS and NOAA. A field visit will be carried out to verify the burnt scar 
area and assess the impact of fire on biomass and peat soil. The Mayas Project shall provide a 
specific SOP as guidance to carry out monitoring of burnt areas. 
3.3.3.2 WRC Monitoring plan 
Water table depths are used as proxy for calculating GHG emissions in the project area, and will 
be monitored by using hydrological modelling approach. Model outputs include daily water table 
depth rasters. Model validity and accuracy will be assured in parameter calibration process by 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
158 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
using directly measured water table depth data. For this purpose, several dipwells will be installed 
in project area and will be measured by combining manual (weekly or two-weekly interval) and 
automatic methods (30-minutes interval) throughout the year. Daily rainfall will be measured using 
two automatic rainfall stations. Map of monitoring devices is shown in Figure 33. Physical 
parameters required for this modeling, namely Hydraulic conductivity and storage coefficient, will 
be measured at random locations, and will be determined later. 
 
Figure 33. Location of various infrastructure and equipment installed in the Project Area, including 
those for monitoring WRC parameters 
This hydrological model will be set-up in a spatially distributed dynamic schematization and will 
include water balance components: rainfall, evapotranspiration, groundwater flow, overland flow 
and open-channel flow. Peat thickness, DTM and rivers and canal networks will act as setup 
parameters. Canal water levels will be used as boundary conditions and will be measured using 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
159 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
water level measurement device (such as a staff gauge or AWLR) by combined manual 
(weekly/two-weekly interval) and automatic methods (30-minute interval). 
Drainage network conditions in the Project Area will be monitored throughout the year. The 
presence or absence of new illegal canals will be mapped and remedied closure the usage of the 
canal by constructing canal blocking as soon as possible. Canal water levels of new illegal canals 
will also be monitored by installing appropriate water level measurement device. 
Daily water table depth rasters resulting from this modelling will be averaged into yearly water 
table depths. From this result, yearly CO2 emissions from peat decomposition can be calculated 
by using the equation given in section 3.2.1.6. 
3.3.4 Dissemination of Monitoring Plan and Results (VCS, 3.18; CCB, CL4.2) 
The Mayas Project will establish a website during the validation and verification process that 
shows the status of implementation activities, milestones and estimated project benefits. 
A summary of the PDD and monitoring reports will be prepared and distributed to stakeholders in 
the Project Zone during public consultation meetings and hard copies of the summary will be 
distributed (see Section 2.3.4 above).  
3.4 Optional Criterion: Climate Change Adaptation Benefits  
The project-zone communities are vulnerable to anticipated climate change impacts because their 
livelihoods and well-being are dependent on the healthy ecosystem of the surrounding peat 
swamp forest in the Project Area. Although some negative impacts from climate change are 
inevitable and beyond the control of the Mayas Project, we aim to strengthen community and 
biodiversity resilience by implementing adaptation options through various project activities. They 
include: 
● Improved coastal protection through provision of training and equipment for mangrove 
restoration and conservation; 
● Restoration of peat swamp ecosystems and reforestation; 
● Planning and designing of climate resilient infrastructural development; 
● Agroforestry capacity building; 
● Adjustment of agricultural calendars, crop patterns and planting practices; 
● Diversification of economic activities by introducing sustainable livelihood options; 
● Capacity building for forest management and NTFP development; 
● Integrated natural disaster management and prevention systems (e.g., early warning 
systems, monitoring protocols, and improved techniques and technologies); 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
160 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
● Disease prevention and control through early warning education and information 
dissemination; 
● Improved access to clean water and sanitation facilities; 
● Improved access to rain/river water collection systems. 
3.4.1 Regional Climate Change Scenarios (CCB, GL1.1) 
The shocks of climate change are already being felt in Indonesia, with more frequent droughts, 
heat waves and floods, and will pose an increasing threat to the country’s development. A World 
Bank (2014) analysis ranked Indonesia 12th among 35 countries that face high mortality risks due 
to multiple hazards, including tsunamis, floods, landslides, droughts, and earthquakes. About 40% 
of Indonesia’s population is at risk of such hazards, and their number will increase under climate 
change which is likely to exacerbate droughts on islands, floods and cyclone intensity across the 
country, and sea level rise effects in coastal areas. Food security and water availability will be 
heavily affected by temperature increase, shorter growing seasons, unpredictable rainfall, and salt-
water intrusion. By 2100, climate change impacts will cost an estimated 2.5-7% of GDP (ADB 
2015).  
More locally, in West Kalimantan, a modelling study of the vulnerability of land and forest fires in 
the province showed that the land use has an impact of 24% on the vulnerability model (Jadmiko 
et al 2017). The results of the vulnerability model analysis show that plantations areas and 
secondary swamp forests are highly vulnerable, particularly on the peat area with depth about 50-
200 cm. The analysis of land and forest fires risk found that the vulnerable areas have high risk 
which is largely unmanaged plantation areas and peatlands (such as the one covered by this 
project). 
3.4.2 Climate Change Impacts (CCB, GL1.2) 
The project zone is likely to exhibit various effects of climate change over the next 50 years with 
greater weather anomalies. Temperatures will increase consistently over the years, and there will 
be a considerable shift in precipitation patterns, evapotranspiration rates, humidity, surface runoff 
and soil moisture levels. Seasonal climate variability is expected to be greater, which suggests a 
substantial increase in rainfall and its intensity for the wet season (December to May), and warmer 
and longer dry months during the dry season (June to November). This is likely to increase fire risk 
in the dry season as well as present a high risk of floods, increased surface runoff, severe 
droughts and heat waves. Within these, the increased fire risk poses the most material threat to 
the project area. Because of climate variability and anomalies, it will be difficult to predict weather 
and seasons in the Project Zone. These extreme weather conditions are anticipated to present 
various impacts on the Project Zone’s environment, economy and society, as shown in the table 
Table 44 below. 
 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4

●    Increased vulnerability to fire damage and smoke inhalation 
●    Reduced quantity and quality of potable drinking water 
●    Increased number of human injuries and deaths 
3.4.3 Measures Needed and Designed for Adaptation (CCB, GL1.3) 
Communities in the project zone are vulnerable to the impacts of climate change (described in the 
section above) because their livelihoods and well-being are dependent on the healthy ecosystem 
of the surrounding peat swamp forest in the project area, including through the provision of 
ecosystem services such as water cycling, soil health and pollination. Although some negative 
impacts of climate change are inevitable, and beyond the control of the Mayas Project, we aim to 
strengthen community and biodiversity resilience by implementing adaptation options through a 
variety of project activities. Those activities most relevant to climate change adaptation, and the 
causal relationship between the activity and the adaptation benefit, are summarised below.

4 COMMUNITY 
4.1 Without-Project Community Scenario  
4.1.1 Descriptions of Communities at Project Start (CCB, CM1.1) 
The Project Zone encompasses 12 village communities and a population of 30,020 people (  
Table 46) as outlined in Section 2.1.15. These villages are considered to be within the Project 
Zone because some of them have administrative areas which overlap with the Project Area; 
because some of the residents of these villages access and utilise natural resources from rivers 
and forests in the Project Area; and because others cultivate lands in areas adjacent to the Project 
Area. Where the Project Area has important subsistence, spiritual and economic value to 
communities, these have also been included as stakeholder communities. The twelve villages vary 
significantly in size from the smallest (Ulak Medang) of 667 people, to the largest (Kuala Tolak) of 
4,280. Malay is the dominant ethnic group in the Project Zone. There are no settlements in the 
Project Area, but settlements in the Project Zone are comprised of simple houses built from timber, 
brick and corrugated iron. Livelihood activities of communities within the Project Zone – for both 
subsistence and income generation – are highly dependent on natural resources. The most 
important natural resource-dependent livelihoods activities are: 
● rice cultivation and agriculture - for subsistence (rice) and some trade in local markets 
(vegetables); 
● non-timber forest products - for subsistence, income generation, medicinal and cultural 
purposes; 
● hunting - for subsistence and income generation; 
● added-value processing of fish products - for subsistence and income generation; 
● fishing and aquaculture - for subsistence and income generation; 
● logging - for subsistence and income generation; 
A small minority of people work as carpenters, tailors, barbers, chicken breeders or as 
entrepreneurs, civil servants or in the private sector. 
Most villagers are fishers and farmers and historically very few have been involved in logging. 
From the late 1970s to the 90s several large logging companies operated in the area, but most of 
the workforce employed came from outside (Sambas District mainly). Some of the main land-uses 
around village settlements include rice cultivation, rubber cultivation, palm oil cultivation, mixed 
agroforestry, mixed vegetable gardens, abandoned (cleared) land and forested land.  

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
164 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
Rice cultivation is practiced by the majority of households in the Project Zone villages and 
includes:  
• Land clearance and preparation - which generally involves the use of a tractor and 
herbicides;  
• Sowing – seedlings are planted immediately once the land has been cleared; 
• Weeding – is carried out regularly to maintain the quality of the rice being planted, but also 
to protect the rice from rats and other pests; and  
• Harvesting - rice ripens 100-120 days after planting and must be harvested immediately or 
the quality decreases. Generally, additional labourers are hired during the harvest season. 
One to two cycles of rice are cultivated in project zone villages per year. Yields are often quite low 
so although widely practiced, rice cultivation is not considered a sufficient source of income and 
often just supplements other types of income generating activities. 
Fishing (including crab fishing) is also widely practiced but mostly by older generations. It requires 
a large amount of capital, to either build or purchase (7-10,000,000 IDR) boats and fishing 
equipment. Younger generations are showing less interest in this livelihood strategy due to the 
barriers (large upfront investment) and modest earnings. 
The great majority (>99%) of villagers belong to the Melayu ethnic group and are majority muslim 
with a small minority of Christians and Confucius followers (<1%). Villages are often divided into 
sub-villages or hamlets (Dusun), for example Sukamaju is divided into Dusun Teratai, Dusun 
Bujang Garang and Dusun Kalimantang. Children have access to primary and secondary 
education and literacy levels among the younger generations are high (80-90%). Access to 
healthcare is possible but is limited by the cost of treatments. About 1-2% of teenage girls drop out 
of school because of unplanned pregnancies. 
Two types of community-level institutions play an important role in the lives of communities in the 
Project Zone: village government and customary institutions. Village government sits within the 
Government of Indonesia’s official administrative structure and is led by the village Head (Kepala 
Desa).   
Information on communities in the Project Zone is derived from participatory research carried out 
by YIARI community development staff as well as a conflict resolution specialist and advisors. This 
combination brought together a range of individuals and institutions with complementary skills, 
including those with in-depth understanding of local culture and practices; researchers formally 
trained in anthropological research methods and participatory rural appraisal techniques; and 
experts in rights-based approaches, sustainable livelihoods, and linkages between conservation 
and community wellbeing. YIARI conducted an initial phase of information collection and produced 
Participatory Rural Assessments for all 12 villages and participatory maps for three (Sungai Putri, 
Ulak Medang and Sungai Awan Kiri), which together overlap with the majority of the Project Area). 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
165 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
These studies describe the historical, socio-economic and cultural ties that communities have to 
the Project Zone/Area. 
Researchers also carried out semi-structured key informant interviews. Unless cited otherwise, the 
community information contained in this PDD is derived from YIARI community development team 
reports. These documents contain rich and detailed information which has been summarised and 
condensed for the purposes of this PDD. Full versions of these reports (in Bahasa Indonesia) will 
be made available on request. The reports can also be translated into English upon request.

4.1.4 Without-Project Scenario: Community (CCB, CM1.3) 
Project Mayas is centred around a logging concession which was destined to be partially selective 
logging and partially converted into a monoculture timber plantation. Logging and conversion into 
a plantation would have meant reduced food security through lack of access to fishing, likely 
reduced agricultural output through the breakdown of water regulation services and an increase in 
fires through the draining and drying out of the peat with associated drop in air quality and 
increased risk for respiratory disease. The baseline scenario would also have led to the loss of all 
non-timber forest products (NTFP) currently utilized by project-zone communities as well as an 
almost certain reduction in availability of fish through the draining of the peat and the degradation 
of freshwater ecosystems. Any hunting would have gradually ceased with ecosystems becoming 
increasingly degraded through logging or replaced by plantations and associated drastic 
reductions in species populations. 
While regulations would compel a licence-holding plantation company to identify cultural areas and 
areas important for food security and ensure protection and access, this practice is widely ignored 
or only partially implemented, putting such areas at risk and communities would likely have lost 
places of cultural significance in the Project Area. 
In the baseline scenario social capital would have been at risk. The typical response to the arrival 
of a large commercial exploiter is the erosion of social cohesion as benefits and costs become 
unequally distributed and factions form. Increased immigration and competition for scarce 
resources further creates opportunities for conflict.  
In the baseline scenario, it is likely that commercial land-use designations would prevail over 
village-based claims. Commercial companies typically base their claims on centrally-created 
tenure maps and do not undergo lengthy participatory mapping to understand customary land-use 
and resource-use patterns in detail. Where conflicts exist the typical response is short-term 
payment at undervalued rates. It is possible that the three villages which started cultivating land in 
the southern portion of the concession would have been evicted.  It is also likely that the baseline 
scenario would have led to a lack of inclusive participation of communities and a lack of 
transparency in decision-making, leading to an opportunity for elite capture in which dominant 
groups typically benefit disproportionately from company payouts, while hindering the flow of 
benefits to marginalized households.  
In the baseline scenario the most likely form of engagement local communities could expect would 
be direct employment. There may be some communication with village-level institutions, but there 
is little precedence among plantation companies for active community engagement in operations 
or decision-making. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
167 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
Finally, in the baseline scenario it is unlikely that community members would benefit from training 
and capacity building opportunities, beyond the potential for specific training given to employees of 
the plantation companies, linked solely to those activities. Local employment is likely to be 
predominantly of unskilled manual labourers.

4.2 Net Positive Community Impacts

4.2.2 Negative Community Impact Mitigation (VCS, 3.19; CCB, CM2.2) 
An estimated 170 households are partially dependent on income from illegal logging within the 
concession. While the project is seeking to halt illegal logging activities, it is mitigating this impact 
by targeting these households for employment in other sectors and for involvement in sustainable 
agricultural activities to ensure the project results in net benefits for these families. 
4.2.3 Net Positive Community Well-Being (VCS, 3.19; CCB, CM2.3, GL1.4) 
Anticipated net well-being impacts of the Mayas Project are shown in Table 48, which include well-
being impacts specific to Gold-level criteria, including climate change adaptation. 
The baseline scenario which would have seen the degradation of all HCV attributes related to 
community wellbeing. Instead, the project will adopt measures consistent with the precautionary 
principle to ensure all participating communities will benefit from project participation substantially, 
mainly through the implementation of a large agro-biodiversity-based livelihoods programme. 
Because the project is based around protecting and restoring a natural peat swamp forest 
ecosystem and preserving the biodiversity it contains, HCV 4, 5 and 6 (as described in 4.1.3) will 
all be protected, maintained and enhanced for communities to continue benefiting long-term from 
hydrological cycle, carbon storage and reduced fire risk services, as well as continued access to 
non-timber forest products, fisheries and cultural sites of significance within the Project Area and 
as far as possible in the rest of the Project Zone as well.
