4.3 Other Stakeholder Impacts  
4.3.1 Impacts on Other Stakeholders (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, CM3.1) 
No offsite stakeholder impacts are anticipated. During the design phase of the project potential 
offsite groups were considered (specifically communities living over 15km away from the 
concession’s border), but none are considered likely to be impacted by the project – indeed, the 
Project Zone itself was designed to incorporate all those groups who were likely to be affected.  
4.3.2 Mitigation of Negative Impacts on Other Stakeholders (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, 
CM3.2) 
Project activities are specifically designed to ensure sustainable community benefits. Such 
activities are implemented in partnership with each community and focus on the development of 
enhanced and sustainable livelihoods: aiming to improve local economies relieves pressure on the 
adjacent natural ecosystem. In parallel, measures will be put in place to ensure a high-level of 
participation of both local communities and local government in project planning and operation, to 
ensure grievances are heard and addressed, and to ensure long-term benefit sharing. 
4.3.3 Net Impacts on Other Stakeholders (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, CM3.3) 
As described in 4.3.1, no offsite stakeholder impacts are anticipated. During the design phase of 
the project potential offsite groups were identified, but none are considered likely to be impacted 
by the project – indeed, the Project Zone itself was designed to incorporate all those groups who 
were likely to be affected.

4.5 Optional Criterion: Exceptional Community Benefits  
4.5.1 Exceptional Community Criteria (CCB, GL2.1) 
The Human Development Index (HDI) classifies Indonesia as a Medium Human Development 
country, with a rank of 108 amongst 169 countries across the world. The Indonesian Bureau of 
Statistics (Badan Pusat Statistik) defines the national poverty line for West Kalimantan Province as 
the minimum purchasing power per capita to be able to afford staple food and non-food items, 
equivalent in cash terms to IDR 454,652 per month. The Mayas Project conducted a social survey 
which found that on average 64% of village households are made up of farmers earning approx. 
IDR 2,000,000 per month. West Kalimantan has an average household size of 4.6 people, making 
the income per capita IDR 434,782. Thus, the Project Zone qualifies as a rural area in which more 
than 50% of households are living under the national poverty line.  
4.5.2 Short-term and Long-term Community Benefits (CCB, GL2.2) 
Anticipated short- and long-term benefits are shown by category of impact in Table 48. 
4.5.3 Community Participation Risks (CCB, GL2.3) 
The Mayas Project seeks to benefit communities through a variety of socio-economic activities 
which also target the most vulnerable and marginalized community members. The project aims at 
reaching these poorer and marginalized communities through a variety of socio-economic 
programmes (see Appendix 2). Furthermore, the project is expected to create a multitude of 
positive economic effects from these programs, as they increase employment opportunities, crop 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
178 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
yields and access to markets. The success of community programs is largely dependent on 
participation, transparent decision-making processes based on mutual trust, and proper 
management of project activities. Three main potential barriers to community benefits in reaching 
the marginalized and/or vulnerable communities were identified, and mitigation measures are 
discussed below. 
● Lack of participation: marginalized poorer communities tend to lack the means or 
time required to attend community meetings. Also, it is common for marginalized 
community members to avoid voicing their opinions in front of a large audience and 
especially in front of powerful groups or individuals. Unbalanced or misrepresented 
target groups for certain project activities could entail non-participation of the poorer 
and marginalized community members. The Mayas Project will encourage all 
community stakeholders, particularly the poorer and marginalized, to participate in 
project activities. Our participatory planning processes enable all project-zone 
communities to be involved in decision-making. Understanding barriers to meaningful 
participation to the project, socialization, information dissemination and community 
meetings take place at various locations and times by considering the needs of the 
marginalized. For example, women-only meetings are facilitated to encourage them to 
voice their opinions, and take place at their houses in the evening when they have 
finished their chores for the day. Community message boards, booklets, flyers and 
videos, and local radio programs will also be used to reach target audiences effectively. 
● Elite capture: A lack of participation and transparency in decision-making processes 
generally creates an opportunity for elite capture. When making decisions regarding an 
infrastructural development project such as road construction, for example, community 
board members may choose a location based on their personal benefits, rather than 
communal benefits as a whole. Without transparent decision-making systems and well-
represented board of communities in place, community programs may be manipulated 
to satisfy the personal interests of certain individuals and may not produce overall 
positive impacts on the marginalized households. In order to address the risk of elite 
capture, the Mayas Project will encourage the poorer and marginalized households to 
participate and aim to enhance the balance of community representation. To increase 
transparency in decision-making processes, meeting records and decisions will be 
maintained and made publicly available. Mixed representation of community members, 
including the marginalized groups, will be encouraged and reinforce more equitable 
and democratic distribution of benefits, thereby placing checks and balances on 
decision-making processes and safeguarding the interest of communities as a whole. 
● Inadequate management of project activities: Another potential barrier to anticipated 
project benefit reaching target community members is inadequate management of 
project activities due to the lack of human and financial resources and effective 
monitoring and evaluation systems. The implementation and progress of project 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
179 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
activities should be regularly monitored in order to assess the impacts of these 
activities on marginalized households, to ensure appropriate allocation and use of 
community funds. Without a stringent system of checks and balances, the risk of the 
elite capture of benefits, ineffective performance and misappropriation of funds remains 
high. The Mayas Project seeks to remove this barrier by supporting to access 
appropriate resources and financial management training to carry out project activities. 
Community-based adaptive management plans will include processes to reinforce 
checks and balances on decision-making processes and lead to a form of democratic 
natural resources governance. 
4.5.4 Marginalized and/or Vulnerable Community Groups (CCB, GL2.4) 
The Mayas Project has identified two marginalized and/or vulnerable community groups: women 
and the poorest households in each community. The project will target these two groups to ensure 
their voices are heard and that they stand to benefit substantially from project activities. Project 
monitoring will attempt to disaggregate results by these groups whenever possible. 
4.5.5 Net Impacts on Women (CCB, GL2.5) 
Community impact monitoring will disaggregate results by gender. 
As discussed in 4.2.1, women are responsible for many of the household chores, but rarely 
contribute financially to running households. As such, they are often excluded from decision-
making, especially at the community level. In order to foster increased gender equality, this project 
will establish women’s livelihoods groups where women will lead initiatives, drawing on existing 
and newly acquired knowledge. The project will support women to form sustainable livelihood 
groups that will focus on designing and leading on the management of multi-purpose tree species 
nurseries for agroforestry and other livelihoods initiatives. Women will also be encouraged to voice 
their opinions, needs and ambitions through focus group discussions, the results of which will be 
channeled back to the village government. In addition, at least 50% of all opportunities for further 
education provided through this project are offered to girls. 
4.5.6 Benefit Sharing Mechanisms (CCB, GL2.6) 
The Mayas Project defines benefit sharing as ‘the intentional transfer of non-monetary benefits 
which improve local wellbeing and support project objectives’. These benefits consist mainly of 
opportunities around education, access to micro-loans, agricultural improvements, aquaculture, 
and other programmmes that aim for long-term improvements in local wellbeing. These community 
programmes are co-designed with the community to ensure long-term impacts, fairness and 
equity. Through regular engagement, benefits will be shared transparently. The sustainable 
livelihoods initiatives and other programmes will have clear monitoring and evaluation schedules 
that will ensure transparency and equity in the allocation of benefits. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
180 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
4.5.7 Benefits, Costs, and Risks Communication (VCS, 3.18; CCB, GL2.7) 
See Section 2.3.1-5. 
 
4.5.8 Governance and Implementation Structures (CCB, GL2.8) 
The Mayas project will support communities in developing skills and providing them with the tools 
to participate in decision-making more effectively through: 
● Taking part in participatory mapping and land-use planning; 
● An on-going Free Prior and Informed Consent process (FPIC), which will continue 
throughout the project to ensure community members have opportunities to voice views, 
wishes, hopes and ambitions regularly;  
● Co-design the type of terms on which the relationship between the project and each 
community is established by providing input, views, and revisions of the MoUs signed with 
the project; 
● Providing genuine opportunities for community feedback on project implementation barriers 
at regular community meetings; 
● Providing genuine opportunities for complaints to be voiced and addressed through the 
grievance redress mechanism. 
4.5.9 Smallholders/Community Members Capacity Development (CCB, GL2.9) 
Project activities to enhance capacity are shown in Appendix 2. Capacity building in relation to 
project governance and participation are described in the section above, 4.5.8. 
In addition, community members will be provided with opportunities to improve skills in five main 
areas:  
• Sustainable agricultural practices  
• Computer literacy 
• English language 
• Fire prevention and fire fighting  
• Wildlife monitoring  
• Mangrove ecotourism (only in relevant villages)

5 BIODIVERSITY 
5.1 Without-Project Biodiversity Scenario  
5.1.1 Existing Conditions (VCS, 3.19; CCB, B1.1) 
The Project Zone is an essential part of the Sentap-Kancang lowland peat-swamp forest 
ecosystem, an area around 57,000 hectares. This region forms a vast landscape along with 
Gunung Palung National Park, Gunung Tarak Protection Forest (Hutan Lindung), and several 
conservation areas in surrounding palm oil plantations, which are disconnected from each other. 
These forest fragments are notable habitat patches for many threatened species such as the 
Bornean Orangutan (Pongo pygmeaus).   
During initial assessments and surveys, the project identified 18 mammal species, 102 birds, 11 
reptiles, 14 amphibians, and >45 plant species (67 floral species identified to genus level). These 
were identified during initial surveys in the Project Area. Using IUCN Red List (2017) data, three 
species are considered Critically Endangered, five are Endangered, and 15 are Vulnerable (YIARI 
report, 2019), 11 species (interim data) are endemic to Borneo, and 15 species are protected 
under Indonesian law (P106/2018, see Appendix 10). Further surveys are almost certain to identify 
more threatened species present at the site. Based on the habitat and nearby records this list is 
likely to expand to include any of the following: 
• Sunda Pangolin (Manis javanica) – CR 
• Kahui/Red Balau (Shorea balangeran) – CR 
• Hairy-nosed Otter (Lutra sumatrana) – EN 
• Bornean Clouded Leopard (Neofelis diardi borneensis) – EN 
• Flat-headed Cat (Prionailurus planiceps) – EN 
• Otter civet (Cynogale bennettii) – EN 
• Storms Stork (Ciconia stormi) - EN 
• White-rimmed Stingray (Fluvitrygon signifier) - EN 
• Asian Arowana (Scleropages formosus) - EN 
• Meranti Semut (Shorea teysmaniana) - EN 
Based on survey work conducted in 2019 and 2020, we estimate the Bornean orangutan 
population size to be between 813-1,204 individuals (YIARI 2018, unpublished report) with density 
estimates of 2.14 – 3.4 individuals per square kilometre. This Bornean orangutan population is the 
biggest population outside conservation areas in West Kalimantan and represents between 1.5 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
182 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
and 2.3% of the remaining global population of this species, making the project area a Key 
Biodiversity Area by the A1 Threatened Biodiversity criterion alone.  
Full details of the biodiversity assessment can be found in (YIARI 2019, unpublished report). Key 
species identified in the survey, including those considered of high conservation value, 
endangered, protected or endemic, are listed in Appendix 10. Measures implemented to protect 
and enhance the site’s biodiversity are discussed further in relation to project activities in Sub-
section 2.1.17, and in relation to the project’s net positive biodiversity benefits in Section 5.2 
5.1.2 High Conservation Values (CCB, B1.2) 
A HCV assessment was conducted within the project area in collaboration with YIARI. Based on 
primary and secondary data that were collected during the identification process, we identified the 
entire Project Area as being HCV 1 (area with notable biodiversity concentration), HCV 2 (large 
landscape-level areas) and HCV 3 (rare and threatened habitats). In addition, several areas 
outside of the Project Area, but within the Project Zone were also identified as HCV 1-3. Each HCV 
value, its current condition, the baseline scenario, the project activity to maintain/enhance and the 
monitoring method and initial values are shown below in Table 49. A map showing HCV 1-3 areas 
within the Project Zone is included above in Section 2.1.16 
5.1.3 Without-project Scenario: Biodiversity (CCB, B1.3) 
The baseline scenario for the project, including commercial drainage, logging and land clearance 
for mono-culture plantation is described in detail in Section 3.1.4 above. The impact of this 
baseline scenario on HCV values and key/trigger species present is given in Table 49 below. 
Beyond the baseline scenario, there is a latent background threat of increased development in the 
region. The provincial and district development plan (RTRWP/K 2015 - 2035) have designated the 
region as a strategically important one for economic growth. This includes Kuala Tolak and Kuala 
Satong. This potentially influences biodiversity conditions in the future due to the increase or 
expansion of settlements, roads, and plantations (Laurance et al 2009). The impact of road 
construction/development will intensify forest conversion and contribute to illegal hunting and 
wildlife trade, thus increase threats to biodiversity (Clements et al 2014). For instance, the 
construction of Ketapang-Tanjungpura road has already provided easy access for illegal loggers 
and poachers to the concession area.

5.2 Net Positive Biodiversity Impacts  
5.2.1 Expected Biodiversity Changes (VCS, 3.19; CCB, B2.1) 
Project activities that are designed to enhance biodiversity, both in terms of HCV values and 
key/trigger species are shown in Table 49. This includes the baseline status (where known) the 
anticipated change and the monitoring approach adopted. 
5.2.2 Mitigation Measures (VCS, 3.19; CCB, B2.3) 
Project activities are designed to consistently protect and enhance HCV areas and so, as a result, 
no negative impacts are anticipated, and no mitigation measures are therefore thought to be 
needed. The impact of project activities on all HCV areas will be constantly monitored and if at any 
point negative impacts are detected then remedial action will immediately be taken.  
Project measures to ensure the maintenance and enhancement of HCVs are described in 5.2.1. 
and Table 49. Project activities will focus on the protection of intact forest and measures targeted 
at reducing illegal and unsustainable hunting. Measures will also include restoration of degraded 
areas and reducing illegal and unsustainable timber extraction and unsustainable and/or illegal 
hunting in the wider Project Zone. Measures will also include mitigating any potential conflict 
between local communities and crop-raiding orangutan. 
5.2.3 Net Positive Biodiversity Impacts (CCB, B2.2, GL1.4) 
The project is anticipated to provide net positive benefits to all biodiversity HCVs and to all 
key/trigger species present. Anticipated project impacts against each of these criteria are shown in 
Table 49. 
5.2.4 High Conservation Values Protected (CCB, B2.4) 
As stated above (5.2.2.), no actions taken during the project will negatively affect HCVs in the 
project area, whereas all project activities are designed to enhance HCV values and to protect the 
population of key and trigger species present.  
5.2.5 Species Used (VCS, 3.19; CCB, B2.5, B2.6) 
Tree species anticipated to be used in reforestation and enrichment planting efforts are listed 
below in Table 51. All species are native to peat swamp forest in West Kalimantan and 
seeds/seedlings will be collected locally.

5.2.6 Invasive Species (VCS, 3.19; CCB, B2.5) 
Only one species, Calliandra calothyrsus, is considered to be a potential problem. This South 
American species is widely planted for animal fodder in Indonesia and considered mildly invasive. 
It is found in the Project Zone and while active efforts to eradicate this species will not take place, 
the project will not plant any individuals of this species as part of its agroforestry and conservation 
agriculture programmes. 
5.2.7 GMO Exclusion (CCB, B2.7) 
No GMOs will be used in the project. 
5.2.8 Inputs Justification (VCS, 3.19; CCB, B2.8) 
The use of chemical fertilizers, pesticides and control agents is not anticipated in the project. 
5.2.9 Waste Products (VCS, 3.19; CCB, B2.9) 
The Mayas Project adopts the principles of Reduce, Reuse and Recycle. Organic waste will be 
separated and composted through village composting initiatives or disposed of through burial. 
Inorganic waste will be separated into recyclable components – which will enter into village- and 
local- government led recycling initiatives. Residual inorganic waste will be removed from the site 
and disposed of through government-run waste disposal facilities in Ketapang. 
5.3 Offsite Biodiversity Impacts  
5.3.1 Negative Offsite Biodiversity Impacts (CCB, B3.1) and Mitigation Measures 
(CCB, B3.2) 
As noted above (5.2.2, 5.2.4), all project activities are designed to deliver positive biodiversity 
impacts, as such, none are anticipated to lead to negative impacts, either on site or off and 
therefore no mitigation measures are likely going to be needed. Increased protection of forest 
within the Project Area could potentially lead to displacement of activities offsite (leakage), 
however this risk is considered low; in part because the extent of HCV forest around the project 
area is limited, but mainly because the project is able to identify the participants of illegal logging 
and hunting, and this group is specifically targeted for support in developing sustainable 
livelihoods, rather than simply displacing their activity elsewhere.  
5.3.2 Net Offsite Biodiversity Benefits (VCS, 3.19; CCB, B3.3) 
As discussed in 5.3.1, offsite negative effects on biodiversity are anticipated to be extremely low, 
or non-existent. In contrast, positive biodiversity benefits within the Project Area and Project Zone 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
193 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
are anticipated to be highly significant. Such benefits are one of the key overarching objectives of 
the project.  
5.4 Biodiversity Impact Monitoring  
5.4.1 Biodiversity Monitoring Plan (CCB, B4.1, B4.2, GL1.4, GL3.4) 
Monitoring criteria for each biodiversity value, including HCVs and trigger species, are shown in 
Table 49 (monitoring method by HCV value/key species) and Table 50 (by method).  
Biodiversity monitoring will focus on the Project Zone’s HCV areas and key species. Monitoring will 
be carried out using a variety of field survey techniques, as shown in Table 50. These include 
camera trapping, orangutan nest surveys, local community interviews to assess hunting levels and 
threats and SMART patrolling. These methods are designed to both collect data regarding the key 
habitats and species as listed in Table 49, but also to collect data across a much wider range of 
species. Rigorous data analysis will then determine whether the Mayas Project has achieved its 
objectives of net positive biodiversity benefits.  
Methods to be employed include: 
• Trained and dedicated survey teams for each survey protocol. 
• Standardized field survey methods.  
• Interview-based survey methods to complement field surveys. 
• Baseline data at each monitoring site, including permanent plots and survey transects. 
• Standardized data analysis and reporting methods for each survey protocol. 
In addition to on-the-ground surveys, data will also be collected through secondary sources (e.g., 
GIS and remote sensing data, third-party studies). Where specialist surveys are required for a 
particular species, the Mayas Project will seek to work in collaboration with outside experts of 
other partners to design and implement such work. 
Biodiversity monitoring will also include aspects relating to climate change adaptation, including 
monitoring the protection and restoration of the peat watershed system, forest cover, fire, and 
reforestation efforts with native ecologically adapted species. 
5.4.2 Biodiversity Monitoring Plan Dissemination (CCB, B4.3) 
Biodiversity monitoring plans will be disseminated along with other information regarding the 
project, as given above in Sections 2.3.1-5. 
5.5 Optional Criterion: Exceptional Biodiversity Benefits  
5.5.1 High Biodiversity Conservation Priority Status (CCB, GL3.1) 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template 
                                                                                               CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
194 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
The project seeks to be validated to the Gold Level for exceptional biodiversity benefits as it fulfils 
criteria for both vulnerability and irreplaceability. 
The project area fulfils vulnerability criterion with the presence of at least 3 Critically Endangered 
species and 5 Endangered species known to occur within the Project Area, and more likely to be 
discovered (see list within Section 2.1.7 above). 
The project area fulfils the irreplaceability criterion as it contains between 1.5 and 2.3% of the 
Bornean orangutan population. This population is resident. Given the scarcity of intact peat-swamp 
forest in this region, we suspect the 1% threshold for irreplaceability is met by a number of other 
endemic species but we do not yet have the data to support this. 
5.5.2 Trigger Species Population Trends (CCB, GL3.2, GL3.3) 
Trigger species (those listed as Critically Endangered, or Endangered) are shown in Appendix 10, 
including population trends (where known) and anticipated with- and without-project scenarios. As 
more trigger species are identified at the site, they will be incorporated to this list.

# 
Mont
h  
Day Location Meeting topic #P Details on activity 
303 16 
Kebun YIARI, 
Sungai Awan Kiri 
Making Bokashi 
fertilizer from chicken 
manure 
6 
1. The initial stage of making compost is 
mixing chicken manure with rice husk and 
bran into a 30 cm compacted layer. 
2. After that the layer is stirred repeatedly by 
re-flushing with 10 Lt of water + EM4 
(effective microorganisms 4) 10 + 10 spoons 
of sugar. 
3. Repeat until raw materials are mixed and 
moist. 
4. The final stage of the bokashi is to tightly 
package it with a tarpaulin for 4 days 
(incubation period). The process of making 
compost takes 14 days.

test the pyrite content in the soil. After 
sampling, the sample is stored in the sample 
box ready for analysis.

382 13,14,22 
Ulak Medang dan 
Tanjung Baik Budi 
Tilapia fish farming 11 
-     Conducting monitoring of the 
development of tilapia fish in Ulak Medang 
and Tanjung Pura 
-     The size of tilapia in Ulak Medang has 
reached 15 to 18 cm. with a water pH of 6.01 
and a temperature of 25° 
-     There was a death of 85% of tilapia fish 
seeds in Ulak Medang due to a lack of 
oxygen in the water and due to the poison 
from palm oil fertilizers that followed the water 
flow from the ditch. 
-     The average weight of the fish is 
around 25 grams/tail 
-     In Tanjung Baik Budi, they are currently 
installing a fence around the pool.

conditions that lack organic matter (infertile 
soil)

APPENDIX 5: LIST OF NATIONAL LAWS 
AND REGULATION RELEVANT TO THE 
PROJECT 
Regulation Name Relevance and Compliancy of the Project 
Law  Number  6  of  2023  on  the  Stipulation  of  
Government Regulation in Lieu of Law Number 
2 of 2022 on Job Creation into Law. 
The  regulation  governs  the  ease,  protection,  
and empowerment of cooperatives and micro, 
small,     and     medium     enterprises,     the     
enhancement  of  the  investment  ecosystem,  
and   the   acceleration   of   national   strategic   
projects, including the improvement of worker 
protection and welfare. 
Law  Number  16  of  2016  on  Ratification  of  the  
Paris    Agreement    on    the    United    Nations    
Framework Convention on Climate Change 
The  regulation  is  an  acknowledgment  of  the  
national commitment to reduce emissions from 
various   sectors,   
forest   conservation,   the   
enhancement  of  renewable  energy,  and  the  
involvement    of    local    communities    and    
indigenous    peoples    in    climate    change    
mitigation. 
Law Number 18 of 2013 on The Prevention and 
Eradication of Forest Destruction 
The purpose of this regulation is to govern the 
Prevention     and     Eradication     of     Forest     
Destruction   by   individuals,   legal   entities,   
and/or  corporations  that  obtain  permits  for  
forest  utilization,  which  results  in  damage  to  
social, cultural, and environmental aspects, as 
well as an increase in global warming. 
Law Number 24 of 2011 on the Social Security 
Organizing Agency (BPJS) 
The  national  social  security  system  is  a  state  
program   aimed   at   providing   certainty   of   
protection and social welfare. 
Law  Number  32  Year  2009  on  Environmental  
Protection and Management; October 3, 2009 
The  purpose  of  this  Law  is  to  create  an  
environmentally     sustainable     development     
through  means  of  an  environmental  planning  
policy,     and     the     rational     exploitation,     
development,      maintenance,      restoration,      
supervision and control of the environment. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
455 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
Regulation Name Relevance and Compliancy of the Project 
Law Number 40 of 2004 on the National Social 
Security System (SJSN) 
The  national  social  security  system  is  a  state  
program   aimed   at   providing   certainty   of   
protection and social welfare. 
Law Number 13 of 2003 on Manpower. The purpose of this regulation is to govern the 
protection   of   labor   and   to   guarantee   the   
fundamental     rights     of     workers/laborers,     
ensuring  equal  opportunities  and  treatment  
without  discrimination  of  any  kind  to  achieve  
the   welfare   of   workers/laborers   and   their 
families while taking into account the progress 
of the business world. 
Law Number 41 of 1999 on Forestry The  purpose  of  this  regulation  is  to  optimize  
the   various   functions   of   forests,   including   
conservation  functions,  protective  functions,  
and production functions, to achieve balanced 
and     sustainable     environmental,     social,     
cultural, and economic benefits. 
Law Number 5 of 1990 on the Conservation of 
Biological    Natural    Resources    and    Their    
Ecosystems 
This     regulation     aims     to     achieve     the     
sustainability  of  biological  natural  resources  
and the balance of their ecosystems in order to 
better support efforts to enhance the welfare of 
the community and the quality of human life. 
Law Number 1 of 1970 on Worker Safety Rules The purpose of this regulation is to ensure the 
safety of every individual at the workplace and 
to  require  that  every  production  resource  is  
used and utilized safely and efficiently.

Government Regulation Number 36 of 2021 on 
Wages. 
This  regulation  governs  wage  policies,  the  
determination  of  wages  based  on  time  units  
and/or output units, wage structure and scale, 
minimum  wages,  lowest  wages  for  micro  and  
small enterprises, wage protection, forms and 
methods of wage payment, items that  can be 
accounted for with wages, wages as the basis 
for   calculating   or   paying   other   rights   and   
obligations, wage councils, and administrative 
sanctions.   Wages   consist   of   the   following   
components: 1) wages without allowances; 2) 
basic  wages  and  fixed  allow
ances;  3)  basic  
wages,    fixed    allowances,    and    variable    
allowances;  or  4)  basic  wages  and  variable  
allowances. 
Government  Regulation  Number  37  of  2021  
concerning the Implementation of the Job Loss 
Guarantee Program. 
This      regulation      governs      participation,      
contributions, benefits, and the organization of 
the Job Loss Guarantee (JKP) program, which 
is integrated into an effective system, funding 
sources,  and  administrative  sanctions.  The  
JKP  is  a  social  security  program  provided  to  
Workers/Laborers who experience termination 
of  employment  in  the  form  of  cash  benefits,  
access  to  labor  market  information,  and  job  
training.  Employers  are  required  to  include  
Workers/Laborers  as  participants  in  the  JKP  
program.  The  JKP  program  is  organized  to  
maintain  a  decent  standard  of  living  when  
Workers/Laborers  lose  their  jobs.  The  JKP  is  
administered  by  the  BPJS  Ketenagakerjaan  
and the central government.

Presidential  Decree  Number  32  of  1990  on  
Management of Protected Areas; July 25, 1990  
The  objective  of  this  Presidential  Regulation  
aims  to  prevent  damage  to  environmental  
sector. The goal is managing the protection of 
the nature, climate, plants and animals and the 
cultural  heritage  as  well  as  the  historical  and  
cultural   values   of   the   nation.   It   seeks   to   
maintain   plant   diversity,   animals,   system   
types, and natural uniqueness. Mayas Project 
use  the  regulati
on  as  the  main  principle  for  
protection of environment and its biodiversity

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
471 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
Source of data  Pinards and Putz (1996) 
Value applied 2.30 
Justification of choice 
of data or description 
of         measurement         
methods               and               
procedures applied  
No specific reference is available on the Residual Stand Damage Factor 
(RSD) for Indonesia case.  
Based on this reason, the RSD value applied in the calculation use the 
RSD value of neighbouring country (Malaysia) as studied by Pinards and 
Putz  in  1996.  The  similarity  in  the  context  of  forest  characteristic  and  
logging  practices  between  both  countries  believed  to  be  relevant  to  
ensure the conservativeness of the calculation.  
Purpose of Data  Determination of baseline scenario  
Comments  -

Data/ parameter:  OF, SLF, WW  
Data unit:  Kg kg-1  
Used in equations:   

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
473 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
Description:  OF, SLF and WW are used for oxidation and the decay of wood product 
in IFM-LtFP  
 
OF = Fraction of wood products that will be emitted to the atmosphere 
between 3 and100 years after production;  
SLF = Fraction of wood products that will be emitted to the atmosphere 
within 3 years of production; and  
WW   =   Fraction   of   extracted   biomass   effectively   emitted   to   the   
atmosphere during production  
 
Wood Waste fraction (WW):  
 
Winjum et al (1998) indicate the proportion of extracted biomass that is 
oxidized  (burning  or  decaying)  from  the  production  of  commodities  for  
developing and developed countries.  
 
Short-lived fraction (SLF)  
Winjumetal1998 give decay rates for proportions of wood products, which 
were converted to with short-term (<3yr)  
 
Additional Oxidized Fraction (OF)  
Winjum et al 1998 gives annual oxidation fractions for each class of wood 
products.  
This gives the additional proportion that is oxidized between the 3rd and 
the 100th.  
Source of data:  The source of data is the published paper of Winjum et al.199838  
Value applied: Wood Waste fraction (WW): 24% (for developing countries) 
 
Short-lived fraction (SLF)

Source Simulated by using stochastic modelling 
Justification Acknowledging stochastic nature of fire incident as well as taken into 
account drainage factor in determining susceptibility to fire

APPENDIX 8: DATA AND PARAMETERS

REDD (APDEG-APD) 
Data / Parameter:  Project Forest Cover Monitoring Map  
Data Unit:  
Description:  Map  showing  the  location  of  forest  land  within  the  project  area  at  the  
beginning  of  each  monitoring  period.  If  within  the  Project  Area  some  
forest  land  is  cleared,  the  benchmark  map  must  show  the  deforested  
areas at each monitoring event  
Source of data:  Remote sensing in combination with GPS data collected during ground 
truthing  
Justification  of  choice  
of  data  or  description  
of            measurement            
methods                 and                 
procedures applied:  
The  minimum  map  accuracy  must  be  90%  for  the  classification  of  
forest/non-forest in the remote sensing imagery.  
If  the  classification  accuracy  is  less  than  90%  then  the  map  is  not  
acceptable for further analysis. More remote sensing data and ground 
truthing data will be needed to produce a product that reaches the 90% 
minimum mapping accuracy.  
Frequency:  Must  be  monitored  at  least  every  5  years  or  if  verification  
occurs  on  a  frequency  of  less  than  every  5  years  examination  must  
occur prior to any verification event  
Any comment:  Where forest land contains more than one forest class, the map must 
be stratified into forest classes using Module X-STR.  
 
Data / Parameter:  Degradation PRA Results  
Source of data:  PRA  
Justification  of  choice  
of  data  or  description  
of            measurement            
The PRA shall consist of semi-structured interviews / questionnaires.  
 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
487 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
methods                 and                 
procedures applied:  
The PRA shall evaluate whether timbe harvest (illegal logging) may be 
occurring in the project area 
 
If ≥ 10% of those interviewed/surveyed believe that degradation may be 
occurring  within  the  project  boundary  then  the  limited  on-the-ground 
degradation survey shall be triggered  
 
An  additional  output  of  the  PRA  shall  be  a  depth  of  penetration  of  
degradation  pressure.  A  maximum  distance  shall  be  recorded  for  
penetration  into  the  forest  from  access  points  (such  as  roads,  rivers,  
already cleared areas) for the purpose of timber.  
 
It  is  likely  that  differing  distances  shall  exist  for  each  degradation  
pressure.  If  multiple  pressures  exist  in  the  same  stratum  the  deepest  
depth of penetration shall be used to define Adeg,i  
 
Frequency: every two years  
Any comment:  Ex ante, an estimation shall be made of degradation in the with-project 
case. If the belief is that zero degradation will occur within the project 
boundaries then this parameter may be set to zero if clear infrastructure, 
hiring and policies are in place to prevent deforestation.

Data/Parameter WTDwps,i,j,t 
Unit m 
Description Yearly average water table depth at stratum i, cell j, year t, in the project 
area, for use as a proxy in estimating CO2 emissions from microbial 
decomposition of peat soils 
Description of 
measurement 
Spatial and temporal distributions of water table depths in a defined 
physical boundary of peatlands are the result of dynamics of water 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
492 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
methods and 
procedures to be 
applied:  
 
balance, and follow established laws of physics. Measured water table 
depths distributed over several locations do not by themselves represent 
the average value(s) from the system, unless unrealistically large 
number of monitoring devices are in place, which makes it out of option 
for this project.  
Water balance modelling is a more appropriate method for estimating 
spatial and temporal distributions of water table depths. Measured water 
table depths from dipwells however can be used as useful initial 
information and indicative values, and, most importantly, as a source of 
data for model calibration. 
Water balance model for this project incorporates the following input-
output system of water: 
Daily rainfall (water input) 
Daily evapotranspiration (water output) 
Groundwater water flow (water output) 
Overland flow (water output) 
Open channel flow (water output) 
Storage change (water balance) 
Governing model parameters/variables include: 
Hydraulic conductivity of aquifer (peat soils) 
DTM 
Storage coefficient of aquifer 
Aquifer thickness 
Boundary condition: 
Water levels in canals 
This water balance model uses the following sub-models: 
Darcy for groundwater flow 
Darcy-Weisbach for overland flow 
Saint Venant for open channel flow 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
493 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
Daily rainfall will be recorded by at least one rainfall station installed in 
the project area, and can be further enriched by using additional 
stations(s) outside project area, such as one from Rahadi Oesman 
Station in Ketapang. Measured water table depths (WTDo) will be 
provided by installing at least 50 dipwells distributed over the project 
area. Water levels of existing canals will be measured by using at least 5 
staff gauges. 
Hydraulic conductivity of peat soils will be sampled at least at 20 random 
locations by using auger hole (Van Bavel & Kirkham) method and/or 
inversed auger hole (Porchet) method. Storage coefficient will be 
sampled at least at 20 random locations by using sample-core 
saturation method. 
This water balance model will be schematized with finite difference 
schematization and will be run in daily time-step. Model output includes 
water table heads (h) i.e., water table height above mean sea level. 
Modelled water table depths (WTDm) were calculated by subtracting 
DTM by h.  
 
For calibration purpose WTDm will be compared against WTDo. 
Parameters storage coefficient, and/or hydraulic conductivity, and/or 
evapotranspiration will be optimized recursively, in such a way, with the 
objective of reducing RMSE between WTDm and WTDo. We accept 
RMSE within 10 percent of WTD range. 
Final result of daily WTDm will be averaged by year, and can be used as 
a proxy for calculating CO2 emissions from microbial decomposition of 
peat soils. 
Frequency of 
recording 
Water table depths will be modelled in daily values, and will be averaged 
by year. Measured water table depths for calibration will be recorded 
using manual dipwells with 2-weekly interval, and with automatic 
dipwells with 30-minutes interval. 
Monitoring 
equipment 
-Manual dipwells use PVC perforated-screened case with millimeter 
scale rod 
-Automatic dipwells use PVC perforated-screened case with HOBO 20-L 
automatic water level logger 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
494 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 
-Manual staff gauges are custom-built using wooden rod and measuring 
scale 
-Automatic staff gauges use PVC perforated-screened case with HOBO 
20-L automatic water level logger 
-Rainfall stations use Davis Weather Station 
Purpose of data Calculation of project emissions 
Calculation method NA 
QA/QC procedures 
to be applied 
Responsible staff will be trained well before carrying out any 
measurement campaign. SOP and standard data sheet will be provided 
and must be mastered by all operators.  
Crucial measurements will be documented with photos and/or videos. 
First-level QC will be performed by field supervisor to ensure 
appropriate entry and to identifying obvious artefacts such as typos, 
misreading, etc. 
Second-level QC will be performed in Bogor for reassuring data quality 
by using manual/visual inspections, standard statistics, comparison 
between wells/stations, trend analysis, etc. 
Comments
